Proxy Divorce and the Forms of Husband's Consent (Ijaza)
Ruling on proxy divorce and consent by word and action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid forms of consent (ijaza) in proxy divorce according to Hanafi scholars, particularly in ambiguous verbal and action-based scenarios.

Core Idea
A stranger's divorce is suspended; consent depends on intention, not mere wording.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into the nuances of proxy divorce. While the default rule is that the husband's consent makes a stranger's divorce effective, the exact wording and actions that constitute valid consent are debated among Hanafi jurists. The text examines specific expressions like 'n'am ma sana't' and 'bi'sa ma sana't', as well as actions such as sending the mahr or accepting the ja'l in khul'. Understanding these disputes sharpens one's ability to apply the principle of ijaza in real cases.

Guided Path
1
Establish the General Rule
State the basic ruling: divorce by a stranger (fuduli) is suspended (mawquf) on the husband's consent (ijaza). If he consents, the divorce occurs; otherwise, it is void. This applies whether the stranger is a man or woman.
This is the foundation from al-Muhit.
2
Analyze the Verbal Dispute
Examine the two key phrases: 'n'am ma sana't' (how well you did) and 'bi'sa ma sana't' (how bad you did). Identify the positions of Shaykh Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl (both are consent) versus the view in al-Qunyah (only one or the opposite, leaning to Abu al-Layth's opinion). Understand the reasoning: 'n'am ma sana't' is often mockery, so not consent.
Focus on the distinction between approval and disapproval tone.
3
Investigate Consent by Action
Consider actions like sending the deferred dower (mahr mu'ajjal) after the divorce or receiving the consideration (ja'l) in khul'. Note the difference of opinion: some say action constitutes consent (like in marriage), others say not because the dower was already obligatory. Differentiate between prompt and deferred mahr.
This affects whether an oath is broken by action alone.
4
Synthesize the Positions
Compile the views of Abu Bakr, Abu al-Layth, and the author of al-Bazzaziyyah. Determine which view seems preferred: the author leans toward Abu al-Layth in one case and notes the need to consult original sources. The practical takeaway is that ijaza is interpreted broadly for verbal consent but cautiously for actions.
No single mufta bihi is given; caution is advised.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن طلاق الفضولين موقوف على إجازة الزوج، فإن أجازه وقع وإلا فلا، سواء كان الفضولي امرأة أو غيرها كما في المحيط. وفي الخانية : رجل قيل له إن فلاناً طلق امرأتك أو أعتق عبدك فقال نعم ما صنع أو بئس ما صنع اختلفوا فيه قال الشيخ الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل . الطلاق فيهما. رجل يقع قال لغيره طلقت امرأتك فقال أحسنت أو قال أسأت على وجه الإنكار لا يكون أجازة، ولو قال أحسنت يرحمك الله حيث خلصتني منها أو قال في إعتاق العبد أحسنت تقبل الله منك كان إجازة ا وإنما لم يكن إجازة في نعم ما صنعت لحمله على الاستهزاء به. ولا فرق بين التنجيز والتعليق، فلو علقه الفضولي بشرط فأجاز الزوج جاز، فلو وجد الشرط قبل الإجازة ثم أجاز لم يقع حتى يوجد الشرط بعد الإجازة كذا في المحيط. وفي القنية : لو طلق امرأة غيره فقال زوجها بئس ما صنعت قال الفقية أبو بكر : هو إجازة ولو قول قال نعم . ما صنعت لا يكون إجازة، وعندي على عكسه وبه أخذ الفقيه أبو الليث لأنه الظاهر ا هـ. وفي البزازية من فصل التعليق بالملك : وتطليق الفضولي والإجازة قولاً وفعلاً كالنكاح فلو حلف لا يطلق فطلق فضولي إن أجاز بالقول حنث وبالفعل لا . ثم قال: قوله (وبالفعل لا قال في النهر يمكن أن يكون بالفعل أن يدفع إليها مؤخر صداقها بعد ما طلقها الفضولي اهـ. قال الرملي ومثل ما في البزازية في فتاوى قاضي ظهير لكن نقل في جامع الفصولين عن فوائد صاحب المحيط أن بعث المهر إليها ليس بإجازة لوجوبه قبل الطلاق بخلاف النكاح. ونقل عن مجموع النوازل في الطلاق والخلع قولين في قبض الجعل هل هو إجازة أم لا فراجعه اهـ . إلا أن يقال: إن ما في جامع الفصولين والمجموع محمول على المهر المعجل فليراجع .
Know that the divorce of strangers is suspended on the husband's consent; if he consents, it occurs, otherwise not, whether the stranger is a woman or someone else, as in al-Muhit. In al-Khaniyah: A man was told that so-and-so divorced your wife or freed your slave, and he said 'How well you did' or 'How bad you did' – they differed about it. Shaykh Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl said: The divorce occurs in both. Another case: a man said to someone else 'I divorced your wife', and he said 'You did well' or 'You did wrong' with the tone of disapproval – it is not consent. If he said 'You did well, may Allah have mercy on you, for you have delivered me from her', or in freeing the slave 'You did well, may Allah accept from you', it is consent. And the reason 'How well you did' is not consent is that it is interpreted as mockery. There is no difference between immediate and conditional; if the stranger makes it conditional on a condition and the husband consents, it is valid. If the condition occurs before consent, then he consents, it does not occur until the condition occurs after consent. Thus in al-Muhit. In al-Qunyah: If someone divorces another man's wife, and the husband says 'How bad you did', al-Faqih Abu Bakr said: it is consent. And if he said 'How well you did', it is not consent. In my view it is the opposite, and al-Faqih Abu al-Layth adopted it because it is apparent. In al-Bazzaziyyah from the chapter of conditional ownership: The divorce of a stranger and consent by word and action are like marriage. If one swore not to divorce, and a stranger divorced, if he consents by word he breaks his oath, but not by action. Then he said: His statement 'and by action no' – in al-Nahr, it could be by action such as giving her the deferred dower after the stranger divorced her. Al-Ramli said: Similarly, in the Bazzaziyyah in the fatawa of Qadi Zuhayr, but it is narrated in Jami' al-Fusulayn from the benefits of the author of al-Muhit that sending the dower to her is not consent because it was obligatory before the divorce, unlike in marriage. And from Majmu' al-Nawazil on divorce and khul', there are two opinions on whether receiving the consideration is consent or not. So check it. Unless it is said that what is in Jami' al-Fusulayn and al-Majmu' is interpreted as the prompt dower (al-mahr al-mu'ajjal), so refer to it.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with proxy divorce (talaq al-fuduli) where a stranger (not the husband) pronounces divorce. The general rule: such divorce is suspended (mawquf) on the husband's consent (ijaza). Consent can be explicit or implied. The text then details various scenarios and disputes among Hanafi scholars. For example, if the husband says 'n'am ma sana't' (how well you did) or 'bi'sa ma sana't' (how bad you did) upon hearing of the divorce, Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl holds both are consent. However, other scholars distinguish based on wording and tone. The text also discusses consent by action, such as sending the deferred dower (mu'ajjal) — whether it constitutes consent. There is a difference of opinion; the author suggests that sending the prompt dower (mahr mu'ajjal) is not consent because it was already obligatory before the divorce. Similarly, accepting the consideration (ja'l) in khul' is disputed. The passage ends with a note that these details should be consulted in the original works.

Key Terms
فضولي (fudūlī): A person who acts without authorization; here, one who pronounces divorce on someone else's wife without being the husband or his agent.
الإجازة (al-ijāza): Consent or ratification by the husband that makes the stranger's divorce effective retroactively.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is a valid consent by the husband in proxy divorce?
A) Saying 'أحسنت' (you did well) without any addition, regardless of tone.
B) Saying 'أحسنت يرحمك الله' (you did well, may Allah have mercy on you).
C) Sending the deferred dower after the divorce.
D) Keeping silent after hearing of the divorce.

Key Takeaway
In proxy divorce, not all words of approval count as consent; intention and context are decisive.

Ambiguous Divorce: Combining Wife with a Non-Subject
Rulings on ambiguous divorce with wife combined with various entities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on ambiguous divorce when the husband combines his wife with a man, ajnabiyya, animal, or dead woman, and identify the intra-Hanafi differences.

Core Idea
When a husband says 'one of you is divorced' to his wife and a non-subject, the wife is divorced; otherwise intention and Imam's view matter.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents several scenarios of ambiguous divorce where the husband links his wife with another entity. The rulings hinge on whether the other entity is a valid subject for talaq. The Hanafi school has internal differences (Abu Hanifa vs. Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad) on some scenarios, making this a rich area for understanding how legal reasoning handles ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the entities combined
For each scenario, list the two entities: wife + man, wife + ajnabiyya, wife + animal/stone, wife + dead woman, wife + invalid marriage.
The wife is always one party; the other varies.
2
Determine if the other is a valid subject for talaq
Recall that a mahall li-l-talaq is only a wife (or certain female slaves). Men, animals, stones, and dead women are not subjects.
This is the key distinction.
3
Apply the rule for non-subject combinations
If the other entity is not a subject, the default is that the divorce falls on the wife (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), unless the husband intends the other. Muhammad differs in some cases.
See animal/stone scenario.
4
Analyze the man and ajnabiyya scenarios
With a man (non-subject), Abu Hanifa says no divorce on wife; Abu Yusuf says yes. With an ajnabiyya (subject), if he says 'tallaqtuhuma', wife divorced; if 'ihdakuma taliq' without intention, Abu Hanifa says no, Abu Yusuf says yes.
Intention and phrasing matter.
5
Consider the exception of hurmah
Even though a man is not a subject, if he says 'ana minki taliq', it creates hurmah (prohibition), making the wife like a stranger.
This is a ruling from al-Muhit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أنه إذا جميع بين منكوحته وغيرها في الطلاق بكلمة فقال إحداكما طالق فهل يقع الطلاق على منكوحته؟ فذكر في الخانية لو جمع بين منكوحته ورجل فقال إحداكما طالق لا يقع الطلاق على امرأته في قول أبي حنيفة، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يقع. ولو جمع بين امرأته وأجنبية وقال طلقت إحداكما طلقت امرأته ولو قال إحداكما طالق ولم ينو شيئاً لا تطلق امرأته. وعن أبي يوسف أنها تطلق . ولو جمع بين امرأته وما ليس بمحل للطلاق كالبهيمة والحجر وقال إحداكما طالق طلقت امرأته في قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وقال محمد: لا تطلق, ولو جمع بين امرأته الحية والميتة وقال إحداكما طالق لا تطلق الحية ا.هـ. ولا يخفي أن الرجل ليس بمحل للطلاق وكذا الميتة فنيبغي الوقوع كما في البهيمة والحجر ولذا قالوا: لو قال أنا منك طالق لا يقع وإن نوى معللين بأنه ليس بمحل له لكن قال في المحيط : إن إضافة الطلاق إلى الرجل وإن لم تصح فحكمه يثبت في حقه وهو الحرمة ولذا لو أضاف الزوج الحرمة فصار كالأجنبية ا.هـ. وفيها أيضاً إذا جمع بين امرأتين إحداهما صحيحة النكاح والأخرى فاسدة النكاح فقال إحداكما طالق لا تطلق صحيحة النكاح كما لو جمع بين منكوحة وأجنبية وقال إحداكما طالق. ولو كان له زوجتان اسم كل واحدة منهما زينب إحداهما صحيحة النكاح والأخرى فاسدة النكاح فقال زينب طالق طلقت صحيحة
Then know that if he includes his wife and another in divorce with one word and says 'One of you is divorced', does talaq fall on his wife? It is mentioned in al-Khaniyah: If he includes his wife and a man and says 'One of you is divorced', divorce does not fall on his wife in the view of Abu Hanifa, and from Abu Yusuf it falls. And if he includes his wife and a stranger woman and says 'I divorced one of you', his wife is divorced. If he says 'One of you is divorced' without intending anything, his wife is not divorced. From Abu Yusuf: she is divorced. And if he includes his wife and something that is not a subject for divorce, like an animal or a stone, and says 'One of you is divorced', his wife is divorced in the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: she is not divorced. And if he includes his living wife and a dead woman and says 'One of you is divorced', the living one is not divorced. End. It is not hidden that a man is not a subject for divorce, and likewise a dead woman, so it should occur as with the animal and stone. Therefore they said: if a man says 'I am divorced from you' it does not occur even if he intends it, because he is not a subject for it. But in al-Muhit: the attribution of divorce to a man, although not valid, its ruling is established in his right, which is prohibition (hurmah). Therefore if the husband adds prohibition, she becomes like a stranger. And in it also: if he includes two wives, one with a valid marriage and one with an invalid marriage, and says 'One of you is divorced', the validly married wife is not divorced, just as if he included a wife and a stranger woman and said 'One of you is divorced'. And if he had two wives both named Zaynab, one valid and one invalid, and says 'Zaynab is divorced', the validly married one is divorced.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, referencing al-Khaniyah, analyzes cases of ambiguous divorce (talaq al-mubham) where the husband links his wife with another entity in one statement. The rulings depend on whether the other entity is a valid subject for talaq. The key scenarios: (1) Wife + man: Abu Hanifa says no divorce for wife because man is not a subject; Abu Yusuf says she is divorced. (2) Wife + ajnabiyya (stranger woman): if 'tallaqtuhuma' (I divorced both), wife is divorced; if 'ihdakuma taliq' (one of you is divorced) without intention, wife is not divorced according to Abu Hanifa, but Abu Yusuf says she is divorced. (3) Wife + non-subject like animal/stone: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say wife divorced; Muhammad says no. (4) Living wife + dead woman: living wife not divorced. The reasoning: the non-subject is not eligible, so by default the statement applies to the eligible wife. There is also an interesting point about a man saying 'ana minki taliq' (I am divorced from you): it does not count as divorce because man is not a subject, but it does create hurmah (prohibition), making the wife like a stranger. Finally, if the husband has two wives with the same name, one valid and one invalid, saying the name divorces the valid one only.

Key Terms
محل للطلاق (maḥall li-l-ṭalāq): An entity that can be subject to divorce: only a wife (or certain female slaves).
بينونة (baynūna): Irrevocable separation; here it implies that the husband's statement creates hurmah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a husband says to his wife and a male stranger 'إحداكما طالق' (one of you is divorced) without intention, what is the ruling?
A) The wife is divorced because she is the only valid subject.
B) The wife is not divorced because a man is not a subject and intention is absent.
C) The man is divorced because he is mentioned first.
D) The divorce is suspended until the husband clarifies his intention.

Key Takeaway
In ambiguous divorce, the identity of the other entity (subject vs. non-subject) and the presence of intention determine whether the wife is divorced, with clear intra-Hanafi differences.

Talaq by an Unauthorized Person (Fuduli) and Its Ratification
Rulings on unauthorized divorce and its ratification by the husband

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of talaq by a fuduli, identify which verbal responses constitute ratification, and apply the rules regarding suspended talaq and the effect of ratification by words vs. actions.

Core Idea
A divorce by an unauthorized person only takes effect if the husband ratifies it, with sincere intention.

This lesson explores the Hanafi ruling on talaq by a fuduli (an unauthorized third party). The core principle is that the husband's ratification is necessary; without it, the divorce does not occur. However, determining what counts as ratification can be subtle, as the passage examines various verbal responses and contextual interpretations. We will also see how this applies to suspended talaq and the effect of ratification on oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
The act is talaq pronounced by a fuduli. The default ruling is that it is not effective unless the husband ratifies it.
This rule applies whether the fuduli is a man or a woman.
2
Distinguish explicit from ambiguous statements
Some verbal responses like 'Ahsanta' (you did well) with clear approval are ratification, while 'N'am ma sana't' (what a good deed) may be mockery and not ratification.
Context and additional words (e.g., 'may Allah accept from you') clarify intention.
3
Recognize the intra-madhhab dispute
There is a disagreement between Abu Bakr and Abu al-Layth on whether 'N'am ma sana't' is ratification. The later preferred view (mufta bihi) follows Abu al-Layth.
This dispute affects fatwa practice.
4
Apply rules for suspended talaq
If the fuduli pronounces talaq suspended on a condition, the condition must occur after ratification for the talaq to take effect.
If the condition occurs before ratification, the ratification does not retroactively validate it.
5
Compare ratification by words vs. actions
Ratification can be verbal or by action. In an oath not to divorce, verbal ratification breaks the oath, while action does not.
This parallels the rule for marriage by fuduli.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إجازة الزوج، فإن أجازه وقع وإلا فلا، سواء كان الفضولي امرأة أو غيرها كما في المحيط . وفي الخانية : رجل قيل له إن فلاناً طلق امرأتك أو أعتق عبدك فقال نعم ما صنع أو بئس ما صنع اختلفوا فيه قال الشيخ الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل . الطلاق فيهما. رجل يقع قال لغيره طلقت امرأتك فقال أحسنت أو قال أسأت على وجه الإنكار لا يكون أجازة، ولو قال أحسنت يرحمك الله حيث خلصتني منها أو قال في إعتاق العبد أحسنت تقبل الله منك كان إجازة ا وإنما لم يكن إجازة في نعم ما صنعت لحمله على الاستهزاء به. ولا فرق بين التنجيز والتعليق، فلو علقه الفضولي بشرط فأجاز الزوج جاز، فلو وجد الشرط قبل الإجازة ثم أجاز لم يقع حتى يوجد الشرط بعد الإجازة كذا في المحيط. وفي القنية : لو طلق امرأة غيره فقال زوجها بئس ما صنعت قال الفقية أبو بكر : هو إجازة ولو قول قال نعم . ما صنعت لا يكون إجازة، وعندي على عكسه وبه أخذ الفقيه أبو الليث لأنه الظاهر ا هـ. وفي البزازية من فصل التعليق بالملك : وتطليق الفضولي والإجازة قولاً وفعلاً كالنكاح فلو حلف لا يطلق فطلق فضولي إن أجاز بالقول حنث وبالفعل لا .
The husband's ratification: if he ratifies it, it occurs, otherwise not, regardless of whether the fuduli is a woman or someone else, as in al-Muhit. And in al-Khaniyyah: A man was told that so-and-so divorced your wife or freed your slave, and he said 'What a good deed' or 'What a bad deed' – they differed on this. The shaykh imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl said: divorce occurs in both cases. A man says to another: 'I divorced your wife,' and he says 'You did well' or he says 'You did badly' with an objection, it does not constitute ratification. If he says 'You did well, may Allah have mercy on you for delivering me from her' or says regarding freeing a slave 'You did well, may Allah accept from you,' it is ratification. And it is not considered ratification in saying 'What a good deed' because it is interpreted as mockery. There is no difference between immediate and suspended [talaq]. So if the fuduli suspends it on a condition and the husband ratifies, it is valid. If the condition occurs before the ratification, then he ratifies, it does not occur until the condition occurs after the ratification – as in al-Muhit. And in al-Qunyah: If someone divorced another's wife and her husband said 'What a bad deed,' al-Faqih Abu Bakr said it is ratification. If he said 'What a good deed,' it is not ratification. And according to me, it is the opposite, and al-Faqih Abu al-Layth adopted this because it is apparent – end. And in al-Bazzaziyyah, from the section of suspension upon ownership: The talaq of a fuduli and its ratification, verbally and by action, is like marriage. So if one swore he would not divorce, and a fuduli divorced, if he ratified by words, he breaks his oath; if by action, he does not.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses the rulings of talaq by a fuduli (unauthorized person) and its ratification (ejaza) by the husband. The core principle is that the husband's approval is necessary; without it, the divorce does not take effect. The text explores various verbal responses and whether they constitute ratification. For example, saying 'you did well' with sincere meaning is ratification, but saying 'what a good deed' is interpreted as mockery and not ratification. Also, if the fuduli suspends the talaq on a condition, the condition must occur after ratification for the divorce to be valid. The text also notes a difference of opinion among later Hanafi scholars on whether saying 'what a good deed' is ratification. Finally, it compares the effect of ratification by words versus actions on an oath not to divorce.

Key Terms
الفضولي (al-fuduli): An unauthorized person who acts on behalf of another without permission, here pronouncing divorce on someone else's wife.
الإجازة (al-ijazah): Ratification or approval by the husband of an unauthorized divorce, making it effective.
التنجيز والتعليق (al-tanjiz wa al-ta'liq): Immediate (unconditional) pronouncement vs. suspended (conditional) pronouncement of divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a fuduli pronounces talaq suspended on a condition, and the condition occurs before the husband's ratification, then the husband ratifies later, does the talaq occur?
A) Yes, as soon as the husband ratifies.
B) No, because the condition must occur after ratification.
C) Yes, because the condition was fulfilled.
D) No, ratification is not effective for suspended talaq.

Key Takeaway
A fuduli's talaq requires the husband's express or implied ratification, and the timing of conditions is crucial for suspended divorces.

Validity of Talaq under Duress
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on talaq under duress, explain the evidence from hadith and istihsan, and distinguish between initiation and confession.

Core Idea
Talaq under duress is valid: the hadith of forgiveness applies to the Hereafter, not worldly legal effects.

The Hanafi school holds that talaq pronounced under coercion is effective, even against the opinion of the three other imams. This ruling is based on a specific hadith and the principle of istihsan (juristic preference). This lesson unpacks the evidence, the rebuttal to the opposing proof, and the practical distinction between initiating divorce and confessing to a past divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position and the opposing view
State the Hanafi ruling that talaq under duress is valid and note that the three other imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold it invalid. Recognize the practical difference: in Hanafi, the divorce takes effect; in other schools, it does not.
The opposing view relies on the hadith of forgiveness for coercion.
2
Analyze the Hanafi evidence (hadith of three things)
The Hanafi proof is the hadith: 'Three things are serious in earnest and in jest: divorce, emancipation, and marriage.' This shows that these actions take effect even when done jokingly, so by greater reason (awla) they take effect under duress.
This hadith is an ahad hadith but accepted by the Hanafi school as sufficient proof.
3
Understand the rebuttal to the opposing hadith
The opponents cite the hadith of forgiveness ('My community is forgiven for error, forgetfulness, and what they are coerced into'). The Hanafi response is that this hadith refers to sin and punishment in the Hereafter, not to worldly legal consequences. The worldly effect (e.g., divorce) still stands.
Consensus (ijma) is that the Hereafter meaning is intended; otherwise, even theft under duress would be excused, which is not the case.
4
Examine the distinction between insha and iqrar and agency
If a person is coerced to initiate divorce (insha), it is valid. If coerced to confess (iqrar) to a past divorce, it does not occur in private (diyanatan) but may occur in court (qada'an). Apply this to agency: if coerced to appoint an agent for divorce, the agency is valid by istihsan, allowing the agent's divorce to take effect.
The istihsan justification is that coercion does not prevent the conclusion of isqatat (relinquishments) like talaq and emancipation.

His statement: '(and if coerced)' meaning even if the husband is coerced to initiate talaq verbally, contrary to the three imams, due to the hadith 'My community is forgiven for error, forgetfulness, and what they are coerced into' (1) and we have what al-Hakim narrated and authenticated: 'Three things are serious in earnest and in jest' as we have mentioned. And what they narrated is from the category of implication (muqtada), and there is no generality of a ruling encompassing both this world and the Hereafter; rather it is either a worldly ruling or an Hereafter ruling, and consensus is that the Hereafter ruling (i.e., punishment) is intended, so the other is not intended with it, otherwise its generality would apply. He (the author) made it absolute, so it includes if he is coerced to appoint an agent for divorce, and he appoints, and the agent divorces, it occurs. And in al-Khaniyyah: A man was coerced by the sultan to appoint an agent to divorce his wife; the husband said, fearing imprisonment and beating, 'You are my agent' and no more, and the agent divorced his wife. Then the principal said, 'I did not appoint him to divorce my wife.' They said: he is not heard, and the divorce occurs, because he uttered the words in response to a command, and the response includes repeating what is in the question – end. And we restricted (the ruling) to initiation, because if he is coerced to confess to divorce and he confesses, it does not occur, just as if he confessed to divorce jokingly or falsely. Similarly in al-Khaniyyah from the section on ikrah, and his intent by 'non-occurrence' in the simile is non-occurrence in private (diyanatan) as per Fath al-Qadir: 'And if he confesses to divorce while being false, it occurs in court' – end. And it is explicitly stated in al-Bazzaziyyah that he may keep her in private if he says, 'I meant by it informing about the past falsely,' even if he did not intend informing about the past. If he intended falsehood or jest, it occurs both in court and in private. And an exception is made in al-Qunyah from occurrence in court if he had taken witnesses beforehand, because... His statement: 'He made it absolute, so it includes if he is coerced to appoint an agent for divorce' – al-Ramli said: and likewise emancipation, as they explicitly stated. As for appointment of an agent for marriage, I have not seen anyone explicitly stating it, but the apparent is that it does not differ from these two, because they explicitly said that triple divorce is valid with coercion by istihsan. Al-Zayla'i mentioned in the issue of divorce that occurrence is istihsan, and by qiyas the agency would not be valid because agency is invalidated by jest, so likewise with coercion, like sale and its examples. The justification for istihsan is that coercion does not prevent the conclusion of a sale but causes it to be void, so likewise agency is concluded with coercion, and void conditions do not affect agency because it is from the category of relinquishments (isqatat). So if it is not invalid, the agent's action is effective. So look at the cause of divorce: you will find it in marriage, so their ruling is one. Ponder his statement: 'and his intent by occurrence in the simile' i.e., in his statement 'just as if he confessed to divorce jokingly or falsely.' However, what is in Fath al-Qadir does not address the joker, only the liar, but the joker is a liar in meaning. His statement 'occurs in court and in private' is similar to what is in al-Khaniyyah: 'it does not occur' like if he confessed jokingly or falsely. Al-Ramli said: But one can interpret what is in al-Khaniyyah regarding the case of falsehood as when he intended to inform about the past, and similarly the expression of Fath al-Qadir is interpreted as... (1) narrated by Ibn Majah in Kitab al-Talaq, Bab 16.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage addresses the validity of talaq under duress (ikrah). The matn states that divorce pronounced under coercion is effective, contrary to the three other imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali). The proof is the hadith of forgiveness for coercion, but the Hanafi response is that this hadith applies to the Hereafter, not worldly legal effects. Instead, they rely on the hadith reported by al-Hakim that three things are serious in earnest and in jest: divorce, emancipation, and marriage. Thus, even under duress, talaq takes effect. The text distinguishes between initiation (insha) and confession (iqrar): if coerced to confess a past divorce, it does not occur (in private), but if coerced to initiate, it does. The discussion extends to agency: if a man is coerced to appoint an agent for divorce, the agency is valid by istihsan, even though qiyas would invalidate it because coercion renders contracts void. The reason is that talaq is an isqat (relinquishment) and such actions are not easily annulled. Similarly, emancipation and marriage under duress are valid. The text also clarifies that if the husband confesses falsely, it occurs in court but not in private if he intended to inform about the past.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Coercion or duress that forces a person to act against his will, here specifically to pronounce talaq.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in certain cases; here used to uphold talaq under duress despite general rules.
الإسقاطات (al-isqatat): Relinquishments or waivers; talaq is considered an isqat of the marital bond, hence less affected by coercion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a husband is coerced to pronounce divorce and does so?
A) The divorce is invalid due to duress.
B) The divorce is valid and takes effect.
C) The divorce is suspended until the husband confirms without duress.
D) The divorce only takes effect in court, not in private.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, talaq under duress is legally effective; the forgiveness hadith applies only to the Hereafter, not to worldly rulings.

Talaq — Divorce

Duress Must Be on Spoken Expression, Not Writing
Ruling that coerced writing of divorce does not take effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on coerced writing of divorce and explain why writing does not effect divorce under duress.

Core Idea
Coerced writing of divorce is invalid because writing only substitutes for speech when speech is impossible.

The passage from Bahr al-Raiq addresses a critical nuance in Hanafi divorce law: duress must be on spoken expression. If a person is coerced to write a divorce, the writing is ineffective. Why? Because writing is only a proxy for speech when there is a necessity—like being unable to speak. Under duress, speech is possible, so the proxy fails. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is writing a divorce under duress. According to Hanafi law, this act does not produce a legal effect: the divorce does not occur (لا تطلق).
Compare with spoken divorce under duress, which is effective.
2
Understand the basis: writing as substitute
Writing is considered a metaphor (majaz) for speech. It is valid only when there is a genuine need (حاجة), such as the inability to speak.
This principle is derived from usage and necessity, not a primary text.
3
Apply the condition: necessity absent under duress
In duress, the coerced person can speak. Therefore, there is no necessity to rely on writing. Hence, writing does not effect divorce.
The absence of necessity is the key distinction.
4
Note the wider principle
Duress must be on spoken utterance for the coerced act to be valid. Coerced writing is treated as if no expression of divorce occurred.
This preserves the primacy of speech in contractual acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاضي يتهمه في إرادته الكذب، فإذا أشهد قبله زالت التهمة والإقرار بالعتق كالإقرار بالطلاق. وقيده البزازي بالمظلوم إذا أشهد عند استخلاف الظالم بالطلاق الثلاث أنه يحلف كاذباً قال : يصدق في الحرية والطلاق جميعاً وهذا صحيح ا هـ . وقيدنا بكونه على النطق لأنه لو أكره على أن يكتب طلاق امرأته فكتب لا تطلق لأن الكتابة أقيمت مقام العبارة باعتبار الحاجة ولا حاجة هنا كذا في الخانية. وفي البزازية : أكره على طلاقها فكتب فلانة بنت فلان طالق لم يقع ا هـ .
The judge suspects him of lying in his intention. If he brings witnesses before him, the suspicion is removed. The acknowledgment of freeing a slave is like the acknowledgment of divorce. Al-Bazzazi restricted it to the wronged person: if he witnesses before the appointment of the oppressor with triple divorce that he swears falsely, he said: he is believed in both freedom and divorce, and this is correct. And we restricted it to being upon speech because if he is coerced to write the divorce of his wife and he writes, she is not divorced because writing is established in place of expression due to need, but there is no need here. As in al-Khaniya. And in al-Bazzaziyya: if he is coerced to divorce her and he writes 'So-and-so daughter of so-and-so is divorced', it does not occur.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the condition that duress must be on spoken utterance for the coerced act to be valid. The discussion begins with the general principle that the judge suspects the coerced person of lying in his claim of duress. If he can bring witnesses to support his claim before the judge, the suspicion is removed. Then al-Bazzazi mentions a specific case: if a person is coerced by an oppressor to swear triple divorce falsely, and he brings witnesses before the judge at the time of the oath, he is believed regarding both his freedom (from the oath) and the divorce not occurring. The author then adds a qualification: the duress must be on spoken speech (النطق), not on writing. The reason is that writing is considered a substitute for verbal expression only when there is a necessity (like the person being unable to speak). In the case of duress, there is no such necessity because the coerced person can speak. Therefore, if a person is coerced to write a divorce and he writes it, the divorce does not take effect. This is supported by references from al-Khaniya and al-Bazzaziyya.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Compulsion by threat of loss of life or severe beating that overcomes the will.
النطق (al-nutq): Spoken utterance, as opposed to writing or gestures.
الكتابة (al-kitābah): Writing the words of divorce or other legal acts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person is coerced to write 'My wife is divorced' and he writes it, does the divorce take effect according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because writing is equivalent to speech.
B) No, because writing substitutes speech only when there is need, and there is none here.
C) Yes, if the duress is severe enough.
D) No, because divorce under duress is never valid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, coerced writing of divorce does not take effect because writing is only a valid expression when speech is impossible.

Refining the Count of Acts Valid Under Duress
Listing and refining the count of acts valid under duress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the acts valid under duress according to the Hanafi school and explain the refinement of the count from 20 to 15 based on textual evidence.

Core Idea
The count of acts valid under duress is refined by merging overlapping categories and excluding inapplicable cases.

The Hanafi school recognizes that certain legal acts remain valid even when performed under duress. However, classical scholars differed on the exact count. This lesson unpacks the critical refinement by the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, who reduces the count by analyzing overlapping categories and excluding cases that do not belong.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial lists
Note the two initial lists: Abu al-Layth's 18 items and the common 10 from most books. Observe the discrepancies.
Al-Khazanah omitted fay', so it becomes 19; then deposit is added, making 20.
2
Analyze the suggested 20
Examine the claim that 20 acts exist. The author verifies that divorce includes suspended, reducing the count to 16.
The author argues that divorce (mutlaq) encompasses suspended divorce, so they are one category.
3
Assess the deposit case
The deposit case from al-Qunyah is scrutinized. The author concludes it is not a valid act under duress but a liability issue, thus excluded.
In al-Bazzaziyya, the depositee does not guarantee, so the act of accepting a deposit under duress is not counted.
4
Finalize the refined count
After excluding the deposit case, the count is reduced to 15. The author also adds further acts later (tawkil, kaffarah, etc.), but the foundational refined set is 15.
The 15 are: talaq, nikkah, ruj'ah, ila', fay', zihar, itq, i'jaab al-sadaqah, 'afw 'an dam amd, islam, qabul al-sulh 'ala mal, tadbir, istilad, rada', yamin, nathr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والنذر. ولم يذكر في الخزانة الفيء فصارت تسعة عشر، ويزاد قبول الوديعة. قال في القنية : أكره على قبول الوديعة فتلفت في يده فلمستحقها تضمين المودع هـ. إن كان بفتح الدال وهو الظاهر فهي عشرون والتحقيق أنها يحتمل الصحة والبطلان، وبالإجازة لا . الكذب صدقاً، وهذا يصير يثبت مع بخلاف إنشاء الطلاق والعتاق ونحوهما مما لا يحتمل الفسخ فإنه لا أثر فيه للهزل على ما سبق اهـ . قوله (والعفو عن دم العمد قال في الكافي : ولو أن رجلاً وجب له على رجل قصاص في نفس أو فيما دونها فأكره بوعيد تلف أو حبس حتى عفا فالعفو جائز ولا ضمان له على الجاني ولا على المكره لأنه لم يتلف له مالاً قوله وقبول المرأة الطلاق على مال قال في الكافي : ولو أكرهت امرأة بوعيد تلف أو حبس حتى تقبل من زوجها تطليقه على ألف درهم فقبلت ذلك منه وقد دخل بها ومهرها الذي تزوجها عليه أربعة آلاف درهم أو خمسمائة درهم فالطلاق واقع ولا شيء عليها من المال، ولو كان مكان التطليقة خلع بألف درهم . كان الطلاق بائناً ولا شيء عليها اهـ. وذكر قبله : لو أكره رجل بوعيد تلف حتى خلع امرأته على ألف ومهرها الذي تزوجها عليه أربعة آلاف وقد دخل بها والمرأة غير مكرهة فالخلع واقع وللرجل على المرأة ألف درهم ولا شيء على الذي أكرهه اهـ. قوله (فهي عشرون) نظمها في النهر فقال :
والمنجز والطلاق على مال والعتق كذلك، والنذر يشمل إيجاب الصدقة فالزائد على العشرة : الإسلام وقبول الصلح والتدبير والاستيلاد والرضاع وقبول الوديعة. وقد أطلق كثير صحة طلاق وإيلاء ظهار ورجعة نكاح مع استيلاد عفو عن العمد رضاع وإيمان وفيء ونذره قبول لإيداع كذا الصلح عن عمد طلاق على جعل يمين به أتت كذا العتق والإسلام تدبير للعبد وإيجاب إحسان وعتق فهذه تصح مع الإكراه عشرين في العد قال : ثم ظهر لي بعد في النظم كذا الصلح معناه كذا قبول الصلح. وقوله «طلاق» معطوف على «الصلح» بعاطف محذوف أي كذا قبول الصلح وقبول الطلاق وحيث كان ما في القنية ليس منها عادت إلى خمسة عشر. وقد أخذت بعض أبيات النهر منها بيتاً مقتصراً على الخمسة عشر فقلت : طلاق وإيلاء ظهار ورجعة نكاح مع استيلاد عفو عن العمد رضاع وإيمان وفيء ونذره قبول لصلح العمد تدبير للعبد وعتق وإسلام فذلك خمسة وعشر مع الإكراه صحت بلا نقد. ونظم صاحب الفتح العشرة التي في أكثر الكتب بقوله : يصح مع الإكراه عـتـق ورجـعـة نكاح وإيلاء طلاق مفارق وفيء ظهار واليمين ونذره وعفو لقتل شاب منه مفارق اهـ. وتممتها بقولي : ما بينه وبين رضاع وتدبير قبول لصلحه كذلك الإستيلاد والإسلام فارق . ثم ظهر لي زيادة أشياء : الأول التوكيل بالطلاق والعتاق استحساناً كما قدمناه عن الرملي. الثاني الكفارة عن الظهار كما في كافي الحاكم من كتاب الإكراه حيث قال : وكذا لو أكرهه على أن ظاهر من امرأته كان مظاهراً، فإن أجبره على أن يكفر ففعل لم يرجع على الذي أكرهه لأنه أمر يلزمه الله تعالى، فإن أكرهه على عتق عبد له بعينه عن ظهاره ففعل عتق ورجع على الذي أكرهه بقيمته ولم يجزه عن الكفارة. الثالث شرط الحنث كما لو قال عبده حر إن دخل هذه الدار فأكره حتى دخل عتق العبد ولا يضمن له المكره قيمته نص عليه في الكافي أيضاً. وفيه أيضاً : وإذا أكره بوعيد تلف حتى اشترى من رجل عبداً بعشرة آلاف درهم وقيمته ألف درهم وعلى دفع الثمن وقبض العبد ولا يرجع على المركه بشيء، وكذا لو أكرهه على شراء ذي رحم محرم منه أو أمة قد ولدت منه أو أمة قد جعلها مديرة إذا ملكها الرابع الخلع كما قدمناه عن الكافي. الخامس الفسخ بالعتق . قال في الكافي ولو أعتقت أمة لها زوج حر لم يدخل بها فأكرهت بوعيد تلف أو غيره على :
In al-Khazanah of Abu al-Layth: The total of what is valid with it (duress) is eighteen things: divorce, marriage, return (ruj'ah), swearing by divorce or freeing, zihar, ila', freeing, obligation of charity, pardon for intentional bloodshed, acceptance by the woman of divorce for money, Islam, acceptance by the killer of reconciliation for intentional bloodshed for money, tadbir, istilad, rada', oath, and vow. And it is mentioned in most books that they are ten: marriage, divorce, return, ila', fay', zihar, freeing, pardon for qisas, oath, and vow. Al-Khazanah did not mention fay', so it becomes nineteen, and the acceptance of deposit is added. He said in al-Qunyah: If he is coerced to accept a deposit and it is lost in his hand, the depositor has the right to hold the depositee liable... if it is with fatha on the dal, which is apparent, then it is twenty. The verification is that it is sixteen because divorce includes the suspended, so there is no discrepancy. Yes, the discrepancy remains regarding the joker... The upshot is that jesting, if it is in the initiation of divorce and similar that does not admit of rescission, the jest is invalidated and what he spoke occurs because he consented to its cause which is entailed by the ruling legally, and therefore it does not admit the condition of option. If it is in acknowledgment and it admits rescission like sale, then the jest is ineffective. And in al-Talwih: Just as the acknowledgment of divorce and freeing under duress is invalidated, so also the acknowledgment of them under jest is invalidated because jest is evidence of lying like duress... His statement (and pardon for intentional bloodshed): He said in al-Kafi: If a man has a right of retaliation against another in life or less, and he is coerced by threat of loss or imprisonment until he pardons, the pardon is valid and there is no liability on the criminal nor on the coercer because he did not destroy property for him. His statement (and acceptance by the woman of divorce for money): He said in al-Kafi: If a woman is coerced by threat of loss or imprisonment until she accepts from her husband his divorcing her for one thousand dirhams, and she accepts that from him, and he has consummated the marriage, and her mahr that he married her for is four thousand dirhams or five hundred dirhams, then the divorce occurs and nothing of the money is due from her. If instead of one divorce it was khul' for one thousand dirhams, the divorce is irrevocable and nothing is due from her. He also mentioned before: If a man is coerced by threat of loss until he divorces his wife by khul' for one thousand, and her mahr is four thousand, and he has consummated, and the woman is not coerced, then the khul' occurs and the man owes the woman one thousand dirhams, and nothing is owed to the one who coerced him. His statement (so it is twenty): He composed it in verse in al-Nahr: ... and the immediate, and divorce for money, and freeing likewise, and the vow includes the obligation of charity. So the excess over ten are: Islam, acceptance of reconciliation, tadbir, istilad, rada', and acceptance of deposit. Many have declared valid: divorce, ila', zihar, return, marriage, along with istilad, pardon for intentional, rada', faith, fay', and his vow, acceptance of deposit, likewise reconciliation for intentional, divorce for consideration, oath by which it comes, likewise freeing and Islam, tadbir of a slave, and obligation of goodness and freeing. So these are valid with duress, twenty in count. He said: Then it occurred to me later that what is in al-Qunyah with kasra on the dal is not among the issues at all. That is because in al-Bazzaziyya he said: If he is coerced by imprisonment to deposit his wealth with this man, and the depositee is also coerced to accept it, and it is lost in his hand, the depositee does not guarantee. I say: It is not hidden that his statement in the verse 'like reconciliation' means like acceptance of reconciliation. And his statement 'divorce' is conjuncted to 'reconciliation' with a deleted conjunction, meaning 'like acceptance of reconciliation and acceptance of divorce.' Since what is in al-Qunyah is not from them, it returns to fifteen. And I took from some verses of al-Nahr a verse confining to fifteen, so I said: ... Then it occurred to me to add things: First, agency (tawkil) in divorce and freeing as istihsan, as we mentioned from al-Ramli. Second, kaffarah for zihar as in Kafi al-Hakim from the book of ikrah where he said: Likewise, if he coerces him to pronounce zihar on his wife, he becomes a muzahir; if he compels him to expiate and he does, he does not recourse against the coercer because it is an obligation from Allah; if he coerces him to free a specific slave of his for his zihar, the slave is freed and he recourses against the coercer for his value, and it does not suffice for the kaffarah. Third, the condition of violation (shart al-hinth): as if he said, 'His slave is free if he enters this house,' and he is coerced until he enters, the slave is freed and the coercer does not guarantee his value, as stated in al-Kafi. Also in it: If he is coerced by threat of loss until he buys a slave from a man for ten thousand dirhams while its value is one thousand, he must pay the price and take the slave, and he does not recourse against the coercer for anything. Similarly, if he coerces him to buy a close relative (dhi rahim mahram) from him, or a slave woman who has given birth to him, or a slave woman whom he had made mudabbara. Fourth, khul' as we mentioned from al-Kafi. Fifth, annulment by freeing (al-faskh bi al-itiq). He said in al-Kafi: If a slave woman who has a free husband with whom he has not consummated is coerced by threat of loss or otherwise to: (the text cuts off).
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the enumeration of legal acts that are valid (sahih) even when performed under duress (ikrah). The author begins by quoting Abu al-Layth's al-Khazanah, which lists eighteen acts. Then he compares with the common ten from most books. He notes that al-Khazanah omitted fay' (return from ila'), so it becomes nineteen. Then al-Qunyah adds acceptance of deposit, making twenty. However, the author (of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) investigates and concludes that the correct count is sixteen because divorce includes both suspended and immediate, so it is one category. He then discusses the ruling for a joker (hazil) and distinguishes between initiation (insha') and acknowledgment (iqrar). After that, he quotes from al-Kafi rulings on pardon for intentional killing and woman's acceptance of divorce for money under duress. Then he returns to the count: the twenty enumerated in al-Nahr are reduced to fifteen after excluding the deposit case. He then presents a poetic summary of fifteen. He then adds five more: tawkil (agency), kaffarah for zihar, shart al-hinth (condition of violation), khul' (already mentioned), and faskh bi al-itiq (annulment by freeing). The passage ends mid-quote about a coerced female slave freeing herself.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Compulsion by threat of loss of life or limb or severe imprisonment that overcomes choice.
الهزل (al-hazl): Jesting or joking, not seriously intending the legal act.
الطلاق على مال (al-talāq ʿalā māl): Divorce in exchange for compensation, akin to khul'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's verification, what is the correct initial count of acts valid under duress after excluding the deposit case?
A) 20
B) 16
C) 15
D) 18

Key Takeaway
The enumeration of acts valid under duress is refined by merging overlapping categories and excluding inapplicable cases, resulting in a foundational count of 15 main acts.

Acts Valid Under Coercion in Hanafi Law
Enumeration of legal acts that remain valid when performed under coercion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the fifteen acts that are valid under coercion in Hanafi law and state the general principle that coercion does not invalidate them.

Core Idea
Coercion does not invalidate these fifteen acts—they take effect even under duress.

In Hanafi law, coercion (ikrah) does not automatically invalidate every legal act. This lesson examines the specific acts—including divorce, emancipation, vows, and settlements—that remain valid even when performed under threat. Understanding this list is crucial for applying the Hanafi position on duress.

Guided Path
1
Recall the principle that some acts are valid under coercion
The Hanafi school holds that certain legal acts, especially those that are unilateral or involve rights of Allah, are effective even if performed under duress. This contrasts with schools like the Shafi'i that require full consent.
The list is not exhaustive but covers common family law and financial acts.
2
Identify the fifteen acts from the author's final list
The passage gives a poetic summary of fifteen acts: divorce, ila, zihar, return to marriage, marriage with istilad, pardon for intentional homicide, fosterage, faith, return from ila, vow, acceptance of settlement for intentional homicide, tadbir, emancipation, and Islam.
Some acts appear multiple times; focus on the unique items.
3
Understand the reasoning for excluding certain acts
The author initially listed twenty acts but later excluded acceptance of deposit (from al-Qunyah) because it is not about the validity of the act but about liability. The true list is fifteen.
This shows the importance of verifying source texts.
4
Compare with the ten-act list from al-Fath
Some earlier scholars listed only ten acts. The author adds five more based on his research. This variation reflects the development of the school.
The ten-act list includes emancipation, return to marriage, marriage, ila, divorce that separates, return from ila, zihar, oath, vow, and pardon.
5
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Suppose a man is threatened with death unless he divorces his wife. According to the Hanafi school, the divorce is valid and takes effect. This is because divorce is one of the listed acts.
Coercion does not negate the legal effect; the act is sahih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق والمنجز والطلاق على مال والعتق كذلك، والنذر يشمل إيجاب الصدقة فالزائد على العشرة : الإسلام وقبول الصلح والتدبير والاستيلاد والرضاع وقبول الوديعة. وقد أطلق كثير صحة طلاق وإيلاء ظهار ورجعة نكاح مع استيلاد عفو عن العمد رضاع وإيمان وفيء ونذره قبول لإيداع كذا الصلح عن عمد طلاق على جعل يمين به أتت كذا العتق والإسلام تدبير للعبد وإيجاب إحسان وعتق فهذه تصح مع الإكراه عشرين في العد قال : ثم ظهر لي بعد ذلك أن ما في القنية بكسر الدال فليس من المواضع في شيء وذلك أنه في البزازية قال : أكره بالحبس على إيداع ماله عند هذا الرجل وأكره المودع أيضاً على قبوله فضاع في يده لا يضمن اهـ . قلت : ولا يخفى أن قوله في النظم كذا الصلح معناه كذا قبول الصلح. وقوله «طلاق» معطوف على «الصلح» بعاطف محذوف أي كذا قبول الصلح وقبول الطلاق وحيث كان ما في القنية ليس منها عادت إلى خمسة عشر. وقد أخذت بعض أبيات النهر منها بيتاً مقتصراً على الخمسة عشر فقلت : طلاق وإيلاء ظهار ورجعة نكاح مع استيلاد عفو عن العمد رضاع وإيمان وفيء ونذره قبول لصلح العمد تدبير للعبد وعتق وإسلام فذلك خمسة وعشر مع الإكراه صحت بلا نقد. ونظم صاحب الفتح العشرة التي في أكثر الكتب بقوله : يصح مع الإكراه عـتـق ورجـعـة نكاح وإيلاء طلاق مفارق وفيء ظهار واليمين ونذره وعفو لقتل شاب منه مفارق اهـ. وتممتها بقولي : ما بينه وبين  رضاع وتدبير قبول لصلحه كذلك الإستيلاد والإسلام فارق .
Book of Divorce. The immediate ( divorce ) and divorce for compensation and emancipation likewise. The vow includes obligation of charity. Beyond the ten are: Islam, acceptance of settlement, tadbir, istilad, fosterage, and acceptance of deposit. Many have declared the validity of divorce, ila, zihar, return to marriage, marriage with istilad, pardon for intentional (homicide), fosterage, faith, return from ila, his vow, acceptance of deposit, likewise settlement for intentional (homicide), divorce for consideration, oath by which it occurs, likewise emancipation and Islam, tadbir of a slave, and obligation of beneficence and emancipation. These are valid under coercion, twenty in number. He said: Then it became clear to me later that what is in al-Qunyah (with a kasra under the dal) is not among these matters at all. That is because in al-Bazzaziyyah it is stated: 'He was coerced by imprisonment to deposit his wealth with this man, and the depositee was also coerced to accept it, and it was lost in his hand; he does not guarantee it.' End. I say: It is not hidden that his statement in the poem 'kadha al-sulh' means 'kadha qabul al-sulh' (similarly acceptance of settlement). And his statement 'talaq' is conjoined to 'al-sulh' with a deleted conjunction, i.e., similarly acceptance of settlement and acceptance of divorce. Since what is in al-Qunyah is not among them, they return to fifteen. And I took some lines from al-Nahr, a line restricted to fifteen, and I said: Divorce, ila, zihar, return to marriage, marriage with istilad, pardon for intentional (homicide), fosterage, faith, return from ila, his vow, acceptance of settlement for intentional (homicide), tadbir of a slave, emancipation, and Islam — that is twenty-five, with coercion they are valid without criticism. And the author of al-Fath organized the ten that are in most books with his statement: 'Valid under coercion: emancipation, return to marriage, marriage, ila, divorce that separates, return from ila, zihar, the oath, his vow, and pardon for intentional killing, the youth from him separates.' End. And I completed it with my statement: What is between him and fosterage and tadbir, acceptance of his settlement, likewise istilad and Islam separate.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement enumerating legal acts that are valid when performed under coercion (ikrah) according to the Hanafi school. The author lists twenty acts initially, then corrects the count to fifteen after excluding the issue in al-Qunyah. He then presents his own poetic summary of fifteen, and later notes that al-Fath listed ten. The list includes divorces, emancipations, vows, settlements, and various family law matters. The key principle is that coercion does not invalidate these acts; they take effect legally. The author clarifies a confusion regarding the term 'acceptance of settlement' and 'acceptance of divorce' in a poem. The passage serves as a memorization anchor for students.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Compelling someone to do an act against their will, typically by threat of harm.
الطلاق على مال (al-talaq 'ala mal): Divorce in exchange for compensation (khul') from the wife.
النذر (al-nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship or charity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Divorce (talaq)
C) Marriage contract without consent
D) Gift

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that fifteen specific acts, including talaq, emancipation, and vows, are valid even when performed under coercion; coercion does not nullify their legal effect.

Five Additional Acts Valid Under Coercion
Additions to the list of coercion-permeable acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five additional acts that are valid under coercion according to the Hanafi school and explain the specific ruling for each, including recourse and validity.

Core Idea
Coercion does not always invalidate; five specific acts remain legally effective by istihsan or textual evidence.

The Hanafi school has a well-known list of acts that are valid despite coercion. Beyond that list, later scholars identified five additional situations where coercion does not void the act. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq collects these extensions and explains the rulings for agency, expiation, conditional emancipation, coerced purchases, khul', and annulment by emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first addition and its basis
The first addition is agency (tawkil) in divorce and emancipation. Note that it is validated by istihsan (juristic preference), overriding the qiyas that would invalidate coerced agency.
Cited from al-Ramli.
2
Distinguish two scenarios for expiation (kaffarah)
The second addition involves expiation for zihar. Coerced expiation is valid but the coerced person cannot recourse. However, if coerced to free a specific slave for the expiation, the slave is freed, the coercer must pay value, but the expiation is not fulfilled.
Key difference: recourse vs. validity of expiation.
3
Analyze the conditional emancipation and coerced purchase cases
The third addition covers conditional emancipation when the condition is met under coercion. The slave is freed and the coercer does not pay value. Also included is coerced purchase at an unfair price; the purchase is valid with no recourse.
Both are from al-Kafi.
4
Recognize khul' as the fourth addition
Khul' (divorce for compensation) is valid under coercion, as previously mentioned from al-Kafi.
The wife must pay the agreed compensation.
5
Note the incomplete fifth addition
The fifth addition concerns annulment by emancipation. The passage is cut off, but it begins with a scenario of a freed slave woman with a free, non-consummated husband being coerced to annul. The full ruling is not provided.
The text ends with 'على :' indicating incompleteness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ظهر لي زيادة أشياء : الأول التوكيل بالطلاق والعتاق استحساناً كما قدمناه عن الرملي. الثاني الكفارة عن الظهار كما في كافي الحاكم من كتاب الإكراه حيث قال : وكذا لو أكرهه على أن ظاهر من امرأته كان مظاهراً، فإن أجبره على أن يكفر ففعل لم يرجع على الذي أكرهه لأنه أمر يلزمه الله تعالى، فإن أكرهه على عتق عبد له بعينه عن ظهاره ففعل عتق ورجع على الذي أكرهه بقيمته ولم يجزه عن الكفارة. الثالث شرط الحنث كما لو قال عبده حر إن دخل هذه الدار فأكره حتى دخل عتق العبد ولا يضمن له المكره قيمته نص عليه في الكافي أيضاً. وفيه أيضاً : وإذا أكره بوعيد تلف حتى اشترى من رجل عبداً بعشرة آلاف درهم وقيمته ألف درهم وعلى دفع الثمن وقبض العبد ولا يرجع على المركه بشيء، وكذا لو أكرهه على شراء ذي رحم محرم منه أو أمة قد ولدت منه أو أمة قد جعلها مديرة إذا ملكها الرابع الخلع كما قدمناه عن الكافي. الخامس الفسخ بالعتق . قال في الكافي ولو أعتقت أمة لها زوج حر لم يدخل بها فأكرهت بوعيد تلف أو غيره على :
Then it appeared to me additional things: First: deputyship in divorce and emancipation by istihsan, as we mentioned from al-Ramli. Second: expiation for zihar, as in Kafi al-Hakim from the book of ikrah, where he said: Likewise, if he coerced him to perform zihar from his wife, he becomes a muzahir; if he compelled him to expiate and he did so, he does not recourse against the one who coerced him because it is a matter obligated by Allah. If he coerced him to emancipate a specific slave of his for his zihar, and he did so, the slave is free and he recourses against the one who coerced him for its value, and it does not suffice for the expiation. Third: condition of incurrence (of penalty), such as if he said: 'My slave is free if he enters this house,' and he was coerced until he entered, the slave is free and the coercer does not guarantee his value; it is stated in al-Kafi as well. And in it also: If he was coerced by a threat of destruction to buy a slave from a man for ten thousand dirhams while his value is one thousand dirhams, and he pays the price and takes the slave, he does not recourse against the coercer for anything. Likewise, if he coerced him to buy a blood relative from him, or a female slave who has given birth to him, or a female slave whom he had made mudabbara when he owned her. Fourth: khul', as we mentioned from al-Kafi. Fifth: annulment by emancipation. He said in al-Kafi: If a female slave who has a free husband who has not consummated with her is emancipated, and then she is coerced by threat of destruction or otherwise to ...
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage lists five additional legal matters that the author discovered are valid under coercion. These are rulings gathered from various Hanafi sources, especially al-Kafi and al-Ramli. The first is agency (tawkil) in divorce and emancipation, validated by istihsan. The second is expiation for zihar: if coerced to expiate, no recourse, but if coerced to free a specific slave for expiation, the slave is freed and the coercer must pay value, though the expiation is not valid. The third is conditional emancipation: if someone made a conditional oath and was coerced to fulfill the condition, the emancipation occurs and the coercer does not pay value. Also included is a case of coerced purchase at an unfair price with no recourse. The fourth is khul' (divorce for compensation). The fifth is annulment by emancipation: a female slave who is married and then freed may choose to annul the marriage (option of puberty), but if coerced to exercise that option, the ruling is cut off in the text.

Key Terms
التوكيل بالطلاق (al-tawkil bi al-talaq): Appointing an agent to pronounce divorce.
الكفارة عن الظهار (al-kaffarah 'an al-zihar): Expiation for zihar (a form of divorce-like pronouncement).
الخلع (al-khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a slave woman with a free husband (non-consummated) is coerced to annul her marriage by emancipation, what is the ruling?
A) The annulment is invalid due to coercion
B) The annulment is valid and she cannot return
C) The passage does not complete the ruling
D) She must pay compensation

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school extends validity under coercion to five additional acts based on later sources, often via istihsan, but the fifth remains incomplete in this passage.

The Drunkard's Divorce in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling on drunkard's divorce with proof and ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce by a drunkard with complete loss of intellect, cite the School's rational proof (dalil aqli), and explain the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
A drunkard's divorce is valid as a stricture from the Lawgiver, even though he lacks intellect.

The Hanafi school holds that a drunkard who has completely lost the ability to distinguish between a man and a woman is still addressed by the Lawgiver with commands and prohibitions. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq unpacks this principle, showing how it leads to the validity of his divorce, despite opposition from some Companions and later scholars.

Guided Path
1
Define the drunkard (sakran) in Hanafi law
Locate in the text the definition: he is one who cannot distinguish a man from a woman nor the sky from the earth. This is the threshold for total loss of intellect.
If some intellect remains, he is treated as sober.
2
Identify the Hanafi ruling and its proof
The passage states that the Lawgiver addressed the drunkard with commands and prohibitions, treating him as a responsible agent. Therefore, his divorce occurs (sahih).
The proof is aqli (rational), not a specific text.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some later Hanafis like Qadi Khan hold non-occurrence. The author follows al-Khulasah and al-Shumnni, affirming occurrence as the mufta bihi.
This shows that even within the school, there is debate.
4
Compare with the opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i and some Companions (e.g., Uthman RA) hold that the drunkard's divorce does not occur. Their proof is a statement from Uthman.
Hanafis do not accept this as binding; they rely on the principle of khitab.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
If a man drinks to the point of losing all discernment and then pronounces talaq, the divorce is valid and counts. If he was coerced to drink, the same applies.
The text explicitly includes coercion as a contingency.

And a drunkard and a mute by his gesture, whether free or slave, due to the absolute (ruling) of a minor, insane, sleeping, and the master regarding Islam of the coerced. And in al-Khaniyyah from al-Siyar, it is restricted that (the coerced Islam is valid) only if the coerced is a harbi; if he is a dhimmi, it does not become Islam. And in al-Qunyah: If he was coerced to divorce his wife three times and he divorced, he does not become a deserter, so no abandonment from him. His statement: 'and a drunkard' means even if the husband is drunk, because the Lawgiver addressed him in his state of drunkenness with command and prohibition regarding a secondary ruling, so we rule that He considered him like one of sound mind as a strictness upon him in secondary rulings. End. And they have interpreted it here according to Abu Hanifa's madhhab: he is one who does not distinguish a man from a woman nor the sky from the earth. If he has some intellect that fulfills obligation, he is like a sober person. The conclusion is that the relied-upon in the madhhab is that the drunkard whose actions are valid is the one who has no intellect to distinguish a man from a woman. And by this, the statement of one who claims that the dispute is only about it in the sense of the opposite of istihsan and istiqbah while distinguishing man from woman is invalidated. And it is amazing what is stated in some expressions that he has intellect that fulfills obligation; undoubtedly on this estimation, no one could say his actions are not valid. And what is in some copies of al-Quduri of restricting the occurrence of the coerced and drunkard's divorce to intention is not the madhhab of our companions, because if he says 'I intended it', it must occur by consensus. And they chose their view in its interpretation regarding the obligation of the hadd punishment, which is that most of his speech is delirium. And they chose regarding the nullification of his purification that he is one whose gait is unsteady, and likewise in his hands that he does not become intoxicated. He made it absolute, so it includes one who drank under coercion or necessity and then divorced. End. Al-Shumnni authenticated it. And Qadi Khan authenticated in his commentary al-Jami' al-Saghir and his Fatawa its non-occurrence, and likewise in Ghayat al-Bayan attributed to al-Tuhfah. And in Fath al-Qadir he said it is the best, and in al-Muhit he said it is good but contrary to consensus, for some said it does not occur whether the drunkard is excusable or not. And among the Companions, some said: If she chooses herself in her session, the entire dower is void from the husband, and there is no liability on the one who coerced her. And if he had consummated before that, the dower belongs to her master from the husband, and the husband does not recourse against the one who coerced her for anything. End. End. And the explanation of the issue in Manh al-Ghaffar is that a harbi can be forced to Islam, unlike a dhimmi. End. But the discussion remains about reconciliation between what is in al-Siyar from al-Khaniyyah and what others have made absolute. Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage examines two distinct issues: the validity of divorce by a drunkard (sakran) and the validity of conversion to Islam under coercion. On the drunkard, the author presents the Hanafi position that a drunkard who has completely lost his intellect (cannot distinguish man from woman, sky from earth) has valid talaq because the Lawgiver addressed him in that state, treating him as responsible to enforce strictness. There is intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: some later scholars like al-Shumnni and Qadi Khan differ. The author notes that Uthman (RA) held it invalid, followed by Shafi'i, Tahawi, etc. But the relied-upon view in the madhhab is that talaq occurs if the drunkard lacks discernment. On the coerced's Islam, the text from al-Khaniyyah restricts validity to a harbi (enemy combatant), not a dhimmi, because a dhimmi cannot be forced to Islam. The author reconciles sources and notes that Ibn al-Shahna reports a difference between qiyas (valid) and istihsan (invalid).

Key Terms
السكران (al-sakran): One whose intellect is overcome by intoxication to the point of not distinguishing a man from a woman.
إسلام المكره (Islam al-mukrah): Conversion to Islam under coercion, valid only if the coerced is a harbi (enemy in war) and not a dhimmi.
الحربي (al-harbi): A non-Muslim enemy combatant who is not under Muslim protection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Always, regardless of his state
B) Only if he was coerced to drink
C) When he has no intellect to distinguish a man from a woman
D) Never, because intoxication removes legal capacity

Key Takeaway
A drunkard's divorce is valid in the Hanafi school because the Lawgiver addresses him even in that state, imposing strictness.

Talaq — Divorce

Coerced Conversion to Islam: Harbi vs Dhimmi
Condition for valid coerced conversion to Islam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition under which coerced conversion to Islam is valid according to the Hanafi school, and explain the distinction between a harbi and a dhimmi.

Core Idea
Coerced conversion to Islam is valid only for a harbi, not a dhimmi, because a harbi may be forced as a war measure.

The Hanafi school accepts coerced conversion to Islam under certain conditions, specifically for a harbi (enemy combatant) but not for a dhimmi (protected non-Muslim). This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies this rule, citing a key text from al-Khaniyyah and reconciling apparent contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling on coerced conversion
The passage begins by implying that coerced Islam is considered valid in some contexts, but then introduces a restriction.
The initial statement seems absolute; the restriction comes from al-Khaniyyah.
2
Find the restriction in al-Khaniyyah
The text quotes al-Khaniyyah: coerced Islam is Islam 'if he is a harbi; if he is a dhimmi, it does not become Islam.'
This is the key condition.
3
Understand the rationale
The reason given in Manh al-Ghaffar: a harbi can be forced to Islam, but a dhimmi cannot because of the covenant of protection.
This is based on the principle that a dhimmi's faith is protected by contract.
4
Reconcile with other texts
The author notes that some other texts state the ruling absolutely without the restriction. The discussion aims to reconcile, but the restricted view is considered the relied-upon one.
Ibn al-Shahna reports that istihsan rejects validity even for a harbi, but qiyas accepts it.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
If a Muslim army captures an enemy combatant and forces him to say the Shahada, his conversion is valid. But if a dhimmi citizen is forced, the conversion is not accepted and he remains a dhimmi.
The ruling emphasizes the difference in status.

The Ruling on Divorce by an Intoxicated Person
Analyzing the validity of divorce by an intoxicated person with intra-Hanafi variations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce by an intoxicated person and evaluate the intra-madhhab dispute among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
Divorce by an intoxicated person is valid as a strictness against the prohibited act of drinking.

The Hanafi school treats the intoxicated person as legally accountable for divorce, even though his intellect is clouded. This strictness serves as a deterrent against consuming intoxicants. However, there is a well-known disagreement within the school: Imam Muhammad holds that divorce occurs, while Abu Yusuf disagrees. The following passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks this dispute, defines the threshold of intoxication, and clarifies the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Define the intoxicated person (sakran)
Identify the Hanafi legal definition: one who cannot distinguish between a man and a woman or between the sky and the earth. This is the threshold for transactions to be valid.
If some intellect remains for taklif, the person is treated as sober.
2
State the core ruling
The Lawgiver addresses the drunkard with commands and prohibitions, so divorce is valid as a strictness. This is the general Hanafi position.
3
Map the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Imam Muhammad says divorce occurs; Abu Yusuf says it does not. The passage notes that the fatwa is given according to Muhammad when the intoxicant is prohibited.
Abu Hanifa's explicit position is not recorded here, but his school's approach implies accountability.
4
Analyze the dalil and rationale
The proof is that the loss of intellect is due to a prohibited act, so strictness is applied. The text also cites the consensus of early scholars that divorce occurs whether coerced or not.
Opponents like al-Shafi'i rely on a narration from 'Uthman that divorce does not occur.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in Fath al-Qadir is that divorce occurs if the intoxicant is prohibited. However, Qadikhan and others prefer non-occurrence. The student must recognize the hierarchy of authorities.
Modern practice may vary; the text emphasizes that the ruling deters drinking.

قالوا : من
يقع في الحالين فمن فرق بينهما كان قوله بخلاف قول الصحابة فيكون باطلاً ! اهـ. وشمل أيضاً من سكر من الأشربة المتخذة من الحبوب والعسل وهو قول محمد. وقال الإمام الثاني : لا يقع. قال في فتح القدير : ويفتي بقول محمد لأن السكر من كل شراب محرم ا هـ . وصحح قاضيخان في فتاوه عدم الوقوع وفي البزازية المختار في زماننا لزوم الحد لأن الفساق يجتمعون عليه، وكذا المختار وقوع الطلاق لأن الحد يحتال لدرثه والطلاق
