الدار أحد فمضت الليلة ولم تفتح لا تطلق ا.هـ. وفي المحيط : سكران قال لآخر وهبت داري هذه منك ثم قال إن لم أقل من قلبي فامرأته طالق ثم أفاق ولم يذكر من هذا شيئاً لا تطلق امرأته لأنه
And an intoxicated person and a mute person by his gesture, whether free or slave, because the 'release' of a child, a madman, a sleeping person, and the master regarding the Islam of one under coercion. And in al-Khaniyya from al-Siyar, it is restricted to being a harbi (enemy combatant); if he is a dhimmi, it is not Islam. And in al-Qunyah: He was coerced to divorce his wife three times, so he divorced; he does not become a deserter, so no abandonment from him. His statement: (and an intoxicated person) meaning even if the husband is intoxicated, because the Lawgiver addressed him in his state of intoxication with commands and prohibitions regarding a subsidiary ruling, thus we consider him like one of sound mind, as a strictness upon him in subsidiary rulings. They have explained it here according to the madhhab of Abu Hanifa: he is one who does not distinguish between a man and a woman, nor between the sky and the earth. If he has some intellect capable of bearing obligation, he is like one sober. The conclusion is that the relied-upon position in the madhhab is that the intoxicated person whose transactions are valid is the one who has no intellect to distinguish between a man and a woman... And by this, the statement of one who claimed that the disagreement is only regarding one who perceives the opposite of istihsan (juristic preference) and repugnance while distinguishing between a man and a woman is invalidated. And astonishing is what is stated in some expressions that he has intellect capable of bearing obligation—there is no doubt that on this assumption no one can say his transactions are invalid. And what is in some copies of al-Quduri of restricting the occurrence of divorce of the coerced and the intoxicated to intention is not the madhhab of our companions, because if he said 'I intended' it must occur by consensus. And in al-Bazzaziyya: Amir al-Mu'minin 'Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'The divorce of the intoxicated person does not occur.' And al-Shafi'i, al-Tahawi, al-Karkhi, and Muhammad ibn Salam adopted this. And they chose his statement in its interpretation regarding the obligation of the hadd punishment... and they chose in the nullification of his purification that he is the one whose gait is disordered, and likewise in his hands that he does not become intoxicated. He made it unrestricted, so it includes one who becomes intoxicated under coercion or under necessity and then divorces. And in al-Khulasa, it is decisively stated that it occurs, reasoning that the loss of intellect occurred by an act that is forbidden in principle, even if it is permissible due to the contingency of coercion, but the cause calling for prohibition exists, so the effect of the existence of the cause applies to divorce. And al-Shumnni corrected it. And Qadikhan in his commentary on al-Jami' al-Saghir and his Fatawa corrected non-occurrence, and likewise in Ghayat al-Bayan attributing to al-Tuhfa. And in Fath al-Qadir, it is said to be the best; and in al-Muhit, it is said to be good, but it contradicts consensus, for some said it does not occur whether excusable or not; and among them are the Companions... [continued]
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the validity of divorce pronounced by an intoxicated person (sakran). The author opens by noting that the Lawgiver addresses the intoxicated person with commands and prohibitions, treating him like one of sound mind as a strictness. He then defines intoxication according to Abu Hanifa: a state where one cannot distinguish between a man and a woman or the sky and the earth. If the person retains some capacity for taklif (legal obligation), he is like a sober person. The relied-upon view is that an intoxicated person whose transactions are valid is one who has completely lost the ability to distinguish. The text then mentions a dispute: Imam Muhammad holds that divorce by one who becomes intoxicated from grain-based drinks or honey drinks occurs, while Abu Yusuf (the 'Second Imam') says it does not. In Fath al-Qadir, the fatwa is given according to Muhammad because the drink is prohibited. Qadikhan favors non-occurrence. However, the preferred view in our time, according to al-Bazzaziyya, is that the hadd punishment is obligatory because wrongdoers gather to drink, and similarly divorce occurs because one is cautious about divorce. The passage also discusses intoxicants like hashish, banj (henbane), and opium: if taken for recreation, divorce occurs; if for medicinal purposes, it does not. The author concludes with various subsidiary cases, emphasizing that the general rule is that divorce by an intoxicated person is valid, but there are exceptions based on the cause of intoxication and the degree of loss of intellect.

Key Terms
سكران (sakran): A person whose intellect is overcome by intoxication to the point that he cannot distinguish between a man and a woman or the sky and the earth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position in Fath al-Qadir, what is the ruling on divorce by a person who becomes intoxicated from a prohibited drink like grain-based alcohol?
A) The divorce does not occur because the person lacked intention.
B) The divorce occurs because the intoxicant is prohibited.
C) The divorce only occurs if the person was coerced.
D) The divorce is suspended until the person becomes sober.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school generally rules that divorce by an intoxicated person is valid, especially if the intoxicant is prohibited, as a strictness to deter consumption.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
Divorce of an Intoxicated Person: Hanafi Rulings
Expanded ruling on validity of divorce by an intoxicated person, covering various intoxicants and intra-Hanafi disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on divorce by an intoxicated person in the Hanafi school, distinguish between valid and invalid cases based on the type of intoxicant and the cause of intoxication, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute between Muhammad al-Shaybani and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
The divorce of an intoxicated person is generally valid in the Hanafi school, but exceptions exist for lawful intoxication and when reason is lost due to other causes.

This lesson discusses the validity of talaq (divorce) pronounced by a person who is intoxicated (sakran). The Hanafi school generally validates such divorces based on istihsan (juristic preference) because the loss of reason does not nullify legal capacity for the consequences of speech. However, there are important exceptions and internal disagreements among the early Hanafi authorities. We will examine the specific rulings for different intoxicants (wine, nabidh, honey drinks, hashish, banj, opium) and the crucial distinction between recreational and medicinal use.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
State the general Hanafi ruling: the divorce of a sakran (intoxicated person) from a prohibited intoxicant is valid (sahih, nafidh) unless an exception applies.
This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) from Muhammad al-Shaybani.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Recognize that Abu Yusuf holds that divorce from drinks made from grains or honey does not occur, while Muhammad al-Shaybani holds it does occur. The fatwa follows Muhammad's view.
The difference stems from the assessment of whether these drinks cause complete loss of reason.
3
Understand the reasoning
The Hanafi school treats all prohibited intoxicants equally because the hadd (fixed punishment) is obligatory for all. The principle of istihsan validates the divorce to prevent further moral harm and to ensure caution.
The hadd itself is a proof (dalil) for the prohibition, and the divorce is a consequence of consuming a prohibited substance.
4
Distinguish exceptions
Identify when the divorce does NOT occur: (1) if the intoxicant is used for a lawful purpose like medical treatment (banj/opium for treatmeant), or (2) if the loss of reason is attributed to another cause not the intoxicant (e.g., headache after drinking wine).
The intention matters: recreational use leads to divorce; medicinal use nullifies it.
5
Apply detailed scenarios
Practice ruling on cases involving an intoxicated agent, conditional divorces, and agency for divorce with property. The validity depends on whether the agent was appointed for a divorce with or without property, and whether the parties were drunk at the time.
These scenarios are from al-Bazzaziyya and other Hanafi texts.

لا يقع، وإن كانا في حال السكر يقع إذا كان بلا مال، ولو كان بمال لا يقع مطلقاً لأن الرأي لا بد منه لتقدير البدل ا هـ. وهو تفصيل حسن .
When the hadd is obligatory, it includes also. They said: whoever falls in both states, and whoever differentiates between them, his saying contradicts the Companions' saying, so it is invalid! End quote. It also includes one who becomes intoxicated from drinks made from grains and honey, and this is the saying of Muhammad. And Imam the Second said: It does not occur. He said in Fath al-Qadir: Fatwa is given according to Muhammad's saying because intoxication from every prohibited drink [is the same] — end quote. Qadikhan corrected in his Fatawa that it does not occur, and in al-Bazzaziyya the relied-upon in our time is the imposition of hadd because the sinners gather around it, and likewise the relied-upon is the occurrence of divorce because for hadd they seek loopholes to avert it, while for divorce they exercise caution, so what is cautionary is preferred. And Sadr al-Islam al-Bazdawi challenged the denier of hadd to distinguish between it and intoxication from permissible things like al-muthallath, and they were unable. Then he said: I found a text from Muhammad regarding him whose mind leaves due to eating hashish and then he divorces — it is called warq al-qinnab. And the scholars of both schools, Shafi'i and Hanafi, have agreed upon the occurrence of his divorce, due to their fatwa of its prohibition and disciplining its seller, even if he says it is permissible then he is a zindiq — thus in al-Mubtagha, and al-Muhaqqiq Ibn al-Humam followed him in Fath al-Qadir. Among those who explicitly declared the prohibition of hashish, banj, and opium is al-Haddadi in al-Jawhara at the end of 'Al-Ashriba', and he declared the punishment of its consumer. It includes also him whose mind leaves by banj and opium — his divorce occurs if he uses it for amusement and intentionally causing harm because it is a sin, but if for treatment then it does not occur due to its absence. From this we say: if he drank wine and then got a headache and his mind left due to the headache, then he divorced, it does not occur because the loss of mind is attributed to the headache, not to the drink. Similarly in Fath al-Qadir, and it is explicit about the prohibition of banj and opium when not for medicine. In al-Bazzaziyya and the rationale calls for its prohibition when not for treatment — end quote. In al-Khaniyya from the book of Khul': All transactions of a drunk person are valid except apostasy, acknowledgment of hadd penalties, and bearing witness to his own testimony. From al-Siyar: This is if he does not know the earth from the sky, but if he knows then his apostasy is valid. In the chapter of Hadd al-Shurb: The transactions of a drunk person from drinks made from grains and fruits — the correct view is that they are not executed, just as they are not executed from one whose mind left by banj. And in al-Yanabi' from al-Ayman: A drunk gave his wife a dirham, and she said to him: 'You will take it back from me when you are sober.' He said: 'If I take it back, you are divorced.' Then he took it immediately while drunk — it does not occur because his speech came as a response to her. In al-Mujtaba: A drunk agent divorced — it does not occur because the harm returns to the principal and it is not permitted — end quote. It is weak, and the correct view is as in al-Zahiriyya from al-Ashriba and al-Khaniyya from al-Talaq: it occurs, unlike if the agent becomes insane and divorces. In al-Qunyah: A drunk knocked on the door and he was not opened for him, so he said: 'If you do not open the door tonight, you are divorced.' Then there was no one in the house, and the night passed and she did not open — she is not divorced. End quote. In al-Muhit: A drunk said to another: 'I have given you this house of mine,' then he said: 'If I did not say it from my heart, then my wife is divorced.' Then he became sober and did not mention any of this — his wife is not divorced because at that time he was in extreme joy, so it appears that he was saying it from his heart — end quote. In al-Bazzaziyya: He appointed an agent for divorce, and the agent divorced while in the state of intoxication — if the appointment was for divorce in exchange for property, it does not occur; if the appointment was while sober and the execution while drunk, it does not occur; if both were while drunk, it occurs if it was without property, and if with property it does not occur absolutely because judgment is necessary to assess the compensation — end quote. It is a good detail.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands upon the ruling in Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the divorce of an intoxicated person. It presents multiple intra-Hanafi disputes and the preferred positions. The author begins by stating that the hadd punishment applies to the drunkard, and then discusses that divorce from a person intoxicated by prohibited drinks (including those from grains and honey) is valid according to Muhammad al-Shaybani, while Abu Yusuf (called 'the Second Imam') holds it does not occur. The relied-upon fatwa, as per Fath al-Qadir, follows Muhammad's view because all prohibited intoxicants are treated equally. However, Qadikhan preferred non-occurrence, and al-Bazzaziyya notes that the chosen position in our time is to impose the hadd punishment due to widespread sin, and also to validate the divorce out of caution. The passage then covers hashish (warq al-qinnab), banj (hemlock-like narcotic), and opium, for which all scholars — both Shafi'i and Hanafi — agree that divorce occurs if used for recreation, because it is prohibited. If used for medicinal purposes, divorce does not occur. The cause of intoxication matters: if the loss of intellect is due to a headache from drinking, the divorce does not occur because it is attributed to the headache, not the drink. The text also details exceptions: a drunk agent's divorce is valid according to the correct view (unlike an insane agent), and a conditional divorce by a drunk person may not occur if the condition is not met or if the speech was a response. The final paragraph discusses a drunk's agency for divorce with or without property, where the validity depends on the presence of compensation and the need for rational judgment. Overall, the Hanafi school validates most divorces by drunk persons, unless the intoxication arises from a permissible cause like treatment, where the divorce fails.

Key Terms
سكران (sakran): A person who has consumed an intoxicant to the point of losing reason.
الحد (al-hadd): The fixed corporal punishment for consuming intoxicants (usually 80 lashes for free men).
الحشيش (al-hashish): A narcotic preparation from cannabis (warq al-qinnab).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the divorce of a person who has consumed banj (a narcotic) NOT occur according to the Hanafi school, as stated in this passage?
A) When he takes it for recreation
B) When he takes it for medical treatment
C) When he takes it intentionally
D) When he takes it while knowing it is prohibited

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates the divorce of an intoxicated person from a prohibited intoxicant taken for recreation, but invalidates it when the intoxication is for a lawful cause or attributed to another factor.

Validity of Divorce by Gesture for a Mute
Ruling on validity of divorce by a mute husband using gesture or writing, with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce by gesture for a mute and explain the role of istihsan in justifying this ruling.

Core Idea
A mute's divorce by gesture is valid because istihsan treats understood gesture as equivalent to speech.

In Hanafi fiqh, a mute person (akhras) is not barred from performing legal acts such as divorce. This lesson examines the ruling that a mute husband's divorce is valid through gesture (isharah), relying on the principle of istihsan (juristic preference). We will explore how a gesture, when understood, is treated like explicit speech, and how this ruling extends to marriage, manumission, and financial transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the parties involved
The act is talaq (divorce) initiated by the husband. The husband is mute (akhras). Determine the default ruling: is divorce by gesture valid?
2
Locate the ruling and the proof
The text states the divorce occurs by gesture (taqa'u bi-isharatihi) because it is understood (mafhumah) and thus like explicit speech (ka al-ibarah) by istihsan. Note that istihsan here overrides any strict qiyas that might require speech.
The proof is aqli (rational) based on istihsan.
3
Identify the scope of the ruling
The same ruling applies to marriage, manumission, sale, and purchase. The gesture is valid whether the mute can write or not, according to the general position.
This shows the generality of the principle.
4
Acknowledge the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some later scholars (mashayikh) hold that if the mute can write, writing should take precedence over gesture. This will be examined in the next lesson.
The dispute does not undermine the basic validity.
5
Summarize the epistemological weight
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah, and the mufta bihi position accepts gesture as valid, with preference for writing if capable. The authority weight is high.
Refer to hanafi_position_box for details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وأخرس بإشارته) أي ولو كان الزوج أخرس فإن الطلاق يقع بإشارته لأنها صارت مفهومة فكانت كالعبارة في الدلالة استحساناً فيصح بها نكاحه وطلاقه وعتاقه وبيعه وشراؤه. سواء قدر على الكتابة أو لا، وقال بعض المشايخ: إن كان يحسن الكتابة لا يقع طلاقه بالإشارة لاندفاع الضرورة بما هو أدل على المراد من الإشارة. قال في فتح القدير : وهو قول حسن ولا يخفى أن المراد بالإشارة التي يقع بها طلاقه الإشارة المقرونة بتصويت منه لأن العادة منه ذلك فكانت الإشارة بياناً لما أجمله الأخرس ا هـ. وإنما ذكر إشارته دون كتابته لأنها لا تختص به لأن غير الأخرس يقع طلاقه بكتابته إذا كان مستبيناً، لا ما لا يستبين، فإن كان على وجه الرسم لا يحتاج إلى النية ولا يصدق في القضاء أنه عنى تجربة الخط ورسمها أن يكتب الله الرحمن الرحيم أما بعد إذا وصل إليك كتابي فأنت طالق، فإن كان معلقاً بالإتيان إليها لا يقع إلا به، وإن لم يكن معلقاً وقع عقيب الكتابة، وإن علقه بالمجيء إليها فوصل إلى أبيها مزقه ولم يدفعه إليها فإن كان متصرفاً في أمورها وقع وإلا لا وإن أخبرها ما لم يدفع إليها الكتاب الممزق. ولو كتب إليها إذا أتاك كتابي هذا فأنت طالق ثم نسخه في كتاب آخر أو غيره فبلغا إليها تطلق تطليقتين ولا يدين في القضاء، ولو كتب إلى
His saying: (And a mute by his gesture) i.e., even if the husband is mute, divorce occurs by his gesture because it becomes understood, so it is like explicit speech in indication by istihsan. Thus his marriage, divorce, manumission, sale, and purchase are valid by it. Whether he is capable of writing or not. Some scholars said: If he is good at writing, his divorce by gesture does not occur because necessity is removed by what is more indicative of intent than gesture. He said in Fath al-Qadir: This is a good saying, and it is not hidden that what is meant by the gesture by which his divorce occurs is the gesture accompanied by some sound from him, because that is his custom, so the gesture is an explanation of what the mute has expressed concisely — end quote. He only mentioned his gesture and not his writing because writing is not exclusive to him; for a non-mute, his divorce by writing occurs if it is legible, not if it is not legible. If it is in the form of a draft, he does not need intention, and he is not believed in court that he intended experimenting with handwriting; his drafting is that he writes: 'In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. To proceed: When my letter reaches you, you are divorced.' If it is conditional upon arrival to her, it does not occur except by it. If not conditional, it occurs immediately after writing. If he made it conditional upon coming to her, and the letter reached her father, who tore it and did not give it to her: if the father is in charge of her affairs, it occurs; otherwise not, even if she was informed, as long as the torn letter was not delivered to her. If he wrote to her: 'When this my letter comes to you, you are divorced,' then he copied it in another letter or other, and both reached her, she is divorced by two divorces, and he is not believed in court. If he wrote to...
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of divorce initiated by a mute husband (akhras). The ruling is that his divorce is effective by gesture (isharah) because the gesture is understood and serves like speech by way of istihsan (juristic preference). This includes his marriage, divorce, manumission, and financial transactions. The text presents a dispute: some scholars say if the mute can write, then gesture is not valid because writing is more explicit. Fath al-Qadir considers that a good opinion, but adds that the acceptable gesture must be accompanied by some sound (taswit), as is customary for mutes. The passage then explains that the matn mentioned gesture and not writing because writing is not exclusive to mutes — a non-mute's divorce by writing occurs if the writing is legible. It details various scenarios of written divorce: if it is a draft (rasm) without clear intention, it does not count; if the divorce is conditioned on the letter reaching the wife, it only occurs upon that condition; if the letter is intercepted by the wife's guardian and not delivered, the ruling depends on whether the guardian has authority over her affairs. Also, if a man writes the same divorce in two separate letters, both arriving, two divorces apply. This passage shows the Hanafi school's nuanced approach to ensuring that the intent of a mute person is properly expressed and effectuated, balancing the means of communication (gesture vs. writing) and the circumstances of delivery.

Key Terms
أخرس (akhras): A person who is mute, i.e., unable to speak, but may use gestures or writing.
إشارة (isharah): A gesture, typically of the hand or head, that conveys meaning. For a mute, it is a valid means of communication for legal acts.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that departs from strict analogy to achieve a more equitable result. Here, it allows gesture to be treated like speech for the mute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what makes a mute's gesture equivalent to speech for divorce?
A) The gesture is written down.
B) The gesture is accompanied by a sound.
C) The gesture is understood.
D) The gesture is witnessed.

Key Takeaway
A mute's divorce by gesture is valid by istihsan because the gesture is understood like speech.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Gesture vs. Writing for the Mute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on gesture vs. writing for mute divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether a mute's gesture is valid if he can write, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Some mashayikh require a mute who can write to use writing, not gesture, but the mufta bihi accepts gesture as valid.

While the basic Hanafi position allows a mute's divorce by gesture even if he can write, some later scholars (mashayikh) argued that writing is more explicit and should replace gesture when possible. This lesson examines that dispute and the opinion of Ibn al-Humam (Fath al-Qadir), who called it a 'good saying' but also added a condition: the gesture must be accompanied by sound. We will analyze the arguments and determine the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
The general Hanafi position (matn) holds gesture valid whether or not the mute can write. Some mashayikh hold that if he can write, gesture is not valid because writing removes the necessity for gesture.
The basis of the latter is that necessity (darurah) is removed by a clearer means.
2
Analyze the reasoning of the mashayikh
They argue that gesture is only allowed because of necessity (since the mute cannot speak). Writing is more indicative of intent (adallu 'ala al-murad) than gesture, so once writing is possible, the necessity ends and gesture should not suffice.
This is a classic application of the principle: necessity ends when the impediment is removed.
3
Evaluate the response from Fath al-Qadir
Ibn al-Humam calls the mashayikh's view 'qawl hasan' (good saying), indicating it has strength. However, he does not overturn the matn; instead, he adds a condition: the gesture must be accompanied by sound (taswit). This refines the general position rather than replacing it.
The condition of sound makes the gesture more closely resemble speech and reduces ambiguity.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that gesture remains valid, but it must be accompanied by sound. If the mute can write, some scholars recommend writing, but both are acceptable. The mushayikh's view is considered but not preferred in fatwa.
Authority: Zahir al-Riwayah generally, with refinement from later scholars.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: If a mute who can writes divorces by gesture without sound, some scholars would invalidate it, but the mufta bihi would accept it if the gesture is clear and accompanied by sound.
This shows how a seemingly minor dispute affects real cases.

Source Text

Written Divorce: Rules and Scenarios
Rules for written divorce by anyone, including conditional delivery and multiple copies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for written divorce, including conditional delivery, legibility, and multiple copies.

Core Idea
Written divorce is valid for anyone (mute or not) if the writing is legible and intent is clear; conditions of delivery affect when divorce occurs.

This lesson moves from gesture to writing as a medium of talaq. The text explains that writing is not exclusive to mutes – anyone can divorce by writing if the writing is legible (mastabin). It then details several scenarios: draft vs. clear writing, conditional upon receipt, delivery to a guardian, and multiple copies. Understanding these rules is crucial for cases where divorce is communicated via letter, email, or other written forms.

Guided Path
1
Understand the baseline: writing is valid for all
The text says writing is mentioned not because it is specific to mutes, but because non-mutes also divorce by writing. The key condition is that the writing must be legible (mastabin). Illegible writing or a draft (rasm) does not count as divorce, and the writer is not believed if he claims it was a practice draft.
The term 'rasm' refers to a draft or practice writing without intention.
2
Distinguish conditional and unconditional divorce
If the letter says 'When my letter reaches you, you are divorced', the divorce is conditional upon arrival. If no condition, the divorce occurs immediately upon writing (or sending, according to some views).
Conditional: 'idha ataka kitabi fa anta taliq'.
3
Analyze the scenario of delivery to the wife's father
If the letter reaches the wife's father who tears it without delivering it, the divorce occurs only if the father is in charge of her affairs (mutasarrif). Otherwise, no divorce occurs even if the wife is informed.
This protects the wife from arbitrary interception.
4
Handle multiple copies
If the husband writes the same conditional divorce in two separate letters and both reach the wife, she is divorced twice. The husband's claim that he intended only one is not accepted in court.
This is based on the apparent meaning of the writing.

Source Text

Fath al-Qadir considers that a good opinion, but adds that the acceptable gesture must be accompanied by some sound (taswit), as is customary for mutes. The passage then explains that the matn mentioned gesture and not writing because writing is not exclusive to mutes — a non-mute's divorce by writing occurs if the writing is legible. It details various scenarios of written divorce: if it is a draft (rasm) without clear intention, it does not count; if the divorce is conditioned on the letter reaching the wife, it only occurs upon that condition; if the letter is intercepted by the wife's guardian and not delivered, the ruling depends on whether the guardian has authority over her affairs. Also, if a man writes the same divorce in two separate letters, both arriving, two divorces apply. This passage shows the Hanafi school's nuanced approach to ensuring that the intent of a mute person is properly expressed and effectuated, balancing the means of communication (gesture vs. writing) and the circumstances of delivery.

Key Terms
مستبين (mastabin): Legible, clear; writing that can be read easily.
رسم (rasm): A draft or practice writing, usually without intention to divorce.
متصرف (mutasarrif): A guardian who has authority over the affairs of the wife, typically her father or guardian.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband sends two separate letters to his wife, each containing 'When this letter reaches you, you are divorced.' Both reach her. How many divorces occur?
A) None, because it is conditional.
B) One divorce, because it is the same divorce.
C) Two divorces.
D) It depends on the husband's intention.

Key Takeaway
Written divorce is valid if legible; conditional divorce depends on fulfillment of the condition; multiple copies result in multiple divorces.

Divorce by Writing: Conditions, Scenarios, and Legal Devices
Multiple rulings on divorce by writing with conditions and alterations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the validity of a divorce by writing by evaluating clarity, format, conditionality, and alterations.

Core Idea
Clear, formally written divorce is binding without intent; alterations or intermediaries can change the outcome.

The written word carries immense legal weight in Hanafi law, especially in divorce. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details numerous scenarios where a written divorce may or may not be valid depending on factors like clarity, formal format, conditional language, erasures, and how the letter reaches the wife. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly applying rulings in both classical and modern contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the writing's clarity and format
First, determine if the writing is 'mustabin' (clear and legible) and whether it follows the official format (ala wajh al-rasm). Official format (e.g., beginning with Bismillah, mentioning the divorce in a standard way) makes intention irrelevant in court; unclear writing requires intention.
Always check the text for explicit wording that signals the format.
2
Check for conditional language (ta'liq)
Look for words like 'if' or 'when' linking the divorce to a future event. If conditional, the divorce only occurs upon fulfillment of the condition. If unconditional, the divorce takes effect immediately upon writing (or upon delivery, depending on the scenario).
Conditional divorce is a form of ta'liq and is valid in the Hanafi school.
3
Examine erasures and alterations
If parts of the letter were erased or rewritten, the ruling depends on where the divorce clause was located relative to other content. The passage provides specific rules for when the divorce is at the beginning, middle, or end.
Erasures can nullify the divorce if the divorce text itself is removed or if the context becomes ambiguous.
4
Consider the role of intermediaries
If the letter is not delivered directly to the wife but to an intermediary (e.g., her father), the divorce is effective only if the intermediary is authorized in her affairs. If he tears the letter without delivery, the ruling depends on authorization and whether the wife is informed.
This scenario highlights the importance of delivery and agency.
5
Apply the ruling to the specific case
Combine all factors: clarity, format, conditionality, erasures, and delivery method. For example, a clear official letter with a conditional statement that is properly delivered and not altered will result in divorce upon the condition's fulfillment.
Each scenario in the passage is an application of these factors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن غير الأخرس يقع طلاقه بكتابته إذا كان مستبيناً، لا ما لا يستبين، فإن كان على وجه الرسم لا يحتاج إلى النية ولا يصدق في القضاء أنه عنى تجربة الخط ورسمها أن يكتب الله الرحمن الرحيم أما بعد إذا وصل إليك كتابي فأنت طالق، فإن كان معلقاً بالإتيان إليها لا يقع إلا به، وإن لم يكن معلقاً وقع عقيب الكتابة، وإن علقه بالمجيء إليها فوصل إلى أبيها مزقه ولم يدفعه إليها فإن كان متصرفاً في أمورها وقع وإلا لا وإن أخبرها ما لم يدفع إليها الكتاب الممزق. ولو كتب إليها إذا أتاك كتابي هذا فأنت طالق ثم نسخه في كتاب آخر أو غيره فبلغا إليها تطلق تطليقتين ولا يدين في القضاء، ولو كتب إلى امرأته كل امرأة لي غيرك وغير فلانة فهي طالق ثم محى اسم الأخيرة ثم بعث بالكتاب لا تطلق وهذه حيلة عجيبة كذا في المحيط. وذكر فيه مسألة ما إذا كتب مع الطلاق غيره من الحوائج ثم محى منه شيأ . وحاصله أنا لحوائج إن كتبها في أوله والطلاق في آخره فإن محى الحوائج فقط فوصل إليها لا تطلق وإن محى الطلاق فقط طلقت. وإن كتب الطلاق أولاً والحوائج آخراً انعكس الحكم، ولو كتب الطلاق في وسطه وكتب الحوائج قبله وبعده فإن محى الطلاق وترك ما قبله طلقت وإن محى ما قبله أو أكثر لا تطلق ولو جحده فبرهنت أنه كتبه بيده وقع قضاء كما في البزازية. وإن كان لا على وجه الرسم نحو أن يكتب إن جاء كتابي هذا فأنت طالق فهذا ينوي ويبين الأخرس نيته بكتابته .
Because a non-mute's divorce occurs by his writing if it is clear, not if it is unclear. If it is in the official manner, it does not need intention, and he is not believed in court if he claims it was a handwriting exercise. The formal manner is that he writes 'In the name of Allah, the Most Merciful, the Most Compassionate, to continue: when this letter of mine reaches you, you are divorced.' If it is conditional upon reaching her, it does not occur except by that condition. If it is not conditional, it occurs immediately after writing. If he makes it conditional upon coming to her, and the letter reaches her father who tears it and does not give it to her, then if the father is authorized in her affairs, the divorce occurs; otherwise not, even if he informs her, as long as the torn letter is not given to her. If he writes to her 'When this letter of mine comes to you, you are divorced,' then copies it in another letter or something else, and both reach her, she is divorced twice, and he is not believed in court. If he writes to his wife 'Every woman of mine except you and so-and-so is divorced,' then erases the name of the last one and sends the letter, she is not divorced, and this is a clever legal device. It is mentioned in al-Muhit. And it mentions the issue when he writes with the divorce other needs, then erases something from it. In summary: if the needs are written at the beginning and the divorce at the end, and he erases only the needs and it reaches her, she is not divorced; if he erases only the divorce, she is divorced. If he writes the divorce first and the needs last, the ruling is reversed. If he writes the divorce in the middle and writes the needs before and after, then if he erases the divorce and leaves what is before it, she is divorced; if he erases what is before it or more, she is not divorced. If he denies it and it is proven that he wrote it with his hand, it occurs judicially, as in al-Bazzaziyya. If it is not in the official manner, such as writing 'If this letter of mine comes, you are divorced,' then he intends and the mute makes his intention clear by his writing.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings on divorce by writing. The key principle is that divorce by writing is valid only if the writing is clear (mustabin) and intended. For official formal writing (ala wajh al-rasm), which follows the custom of writing a divorce letter, intention is not required for validity in court—the writing itself is evidence. For non-official writing, intention is necessary. The passage explores various scenarios: conditional divorce (ta'liq) upon receipt of the letter, erasing parts of the letter, and the effect of an intermediary (like the wife's father). A notable 'hila' (legal device) is mentioned: if a man writes that all his wives except a named one are divorced, then erases that name, the divorce of the named wife does not occur. The rulings also cover when a letter is copied and both reach her (two divorces), and when a letter is torn by the father. The main takeaway is that the written word carries great weight in divorce, and alterations can change the outcome. The final part addresses the mute's writing: if he writes in a non-official manner, his intention is considered.

Key Terms
على وجه الرسم (ala wajh al-rasm): In the formal/official manner of writing a divorce letter, following a customary format.
مستبين (mustabin): Clear and legible writing that can be understood.
معلق (mu'allaq): Conditional divorce where the occurrence of divorce depends upon a future event.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man writes to his wife: 'When my letter reaches you, you are divorced.' He then copies the same text in another letter and both reach her. How many divorces occur?
A) One divorce
B) Two divorces
C) No divorce
D) It depends on his intention

Key Takeaway
The validity of a written divorce hinges on clarity, formal format, fulfillment of conditions, and unaltered delivery to the wife or her authorized agent.

Condition of Permanence for a Mute's Gesture
Defines the condition of permanence for a mute's gesture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between a permanently mute person and a person with a temporary speech impediment for the validity of divorce by gesture, and state the Hanafi ruling for each case.

Core Idea
A mute's gesture is valid for divorce only if the muteness is permanent; temporary impediments require suspension until death.

The Hanafi school permits a mute person to express divorce through gestures, but this concession is conditional. This passage clarifies that the muteness must be permanent—either from birth or acquired and lasting—to validate the gesture. For those with temporary speech impediments, the divorce is suspended, taking effect only if the condition persists until death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage distinguishes between a permanent mute (akhras) and someone with a temporary speech impediment (i'tiqal lisan). Note the different rulings.
2
State the ruling for each
For a permanent mute, gesture is valid. For the temporary impediment, divorce is suspended (muwaqqaf): it takes effect only if the condition lasts until death; if the person recovers, it becomes invalid.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on duration
Al-Timirtashi specified one year as the threshold for permanence, but the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that muteness must last until death for the gesture to be valid.
This difference affects cases where the person recovers after a year but before death.
4
Apply the mufta bihi
In practice, the fatwa is that only those born mute or with muteness lasting until death can validly divorce by gesture. Temporary impediments require waiting until death or recovery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد صاحب الينابيع الأخرس بكونه ولد أخرس أو طرأ عليه ودام وإن لم يدم لا يقع طلاقه. وقدر التمرتاشي الامتداد هنا بسنة، وذكر الحاكم أبو محمد رواية عن أبي حنيفة فقال : إن دامت العقلة إلى وقت الموت يجوز إقراره بالإشارة ويجوز الإشهاد عليه لأنه عجز عن النطق بمعنى لا كالأخرس. قال الشارح في آخر الكتاب قالوا وعليه الفتوى ا.هـ. فعلى هذا إذا طلق من اعتقل لسانه توقف فإن دام به إلى الموت نفذ وإن زال بطل
The author of al-Yanabi' restricted the mute to one who was born mute or muteness occurred and continued; if it did not continue, his divorce does not occur. Al-Timirtashi estimated the duration here as one year. Al-Hakim Abu Muhammad mentioned a narration from Abu Hanifa saying: If the speech impediment continues until the time of death, his acknowledgment by gesture is permissible and witnessing against him is permissible because he is unable to speak in a way that is not like the mute. The commentator said at the end of the book: They said, and upon it is the fatwa. So on this basis, if one whose tongue is tied divorces, it is suspended; if it continues until death, it takes effect, and if it ceases, it is invalid.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage defines the condition for a mute's gesture to be valid: the muteness must be permanent, either from birth or acquired and lasting. Al-Timirtashi specifies a duration of one year as a criterion for permanence. There is a narration from Abu Hanifa that for a person with a temporary speech impediment (i'tiqal lisan), the divorce is suspended (muwaqqaf) pending the outcome: if the impediment continues until death, the divorce takes effect; if the person recovers, the divorce is void. The relied-upon fatwa (al-mu'tamad) is that the mute's gesture is only valid if the muteness is permanent. This distinction prevents temporary conditions from being treated as permanent muteness.

Key Terms
أخرس (akhras): A person who is mute from birth or due to a permanent condition.
اعتقل لسانه (i'taqala lisanuhu): A person whose tongue is tied temporarily, causing speech impediment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The divorce is valid anyway
B) The divorce is invalidated
C) It is valid if it was written
D) It requires a new divorce

Key Takeaway
For gestural divorce to be valid, the mute's condition must be permanent; temporary impediments lead to suspended divorce that takes effect only if the condition persists until death.

Divorce Applies to Free and Slave Men
Ruling that divorce is valid from both free and slave men, with hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of divorce from a slave husband and cite the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
A slave’s divorce is valid because the right to divorce belongs to the husband, whether free or slave.

This lesson examines a concise ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq affirming that divorce is equally valid when issued by a free man or a slave. The Hanafi school relies on general proofs and a specific hadith to establish this. Understanding this ruling clarifies that marriage authority, not legal freedom, grounds the power of divorce.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the act and its legal status: divorce (talaq) from a slave husband is valid and effective, just as from a free man.
Focus on the equality of free and slave status.
2
Identify the evidence
Locate the Hadith cited: 'al-talaqu liman akhadha bi al-saq' (Divorce belongs to the one who takes hold of the leg). This is from Ibn Majah and al-Daraqutni.
The Hadith is ahab, but accepted by the Hanafi school.
3
Interpret the idiom
Understand the metaphor: 'taking hold of the leg' refers to the husband who has authority over his wife. The Hadith thus attributes the right of divorce to the husband unconditionally.
Literally, it means the one who holds the leg (in intimacy or authority).
4
Derive the principle
The right to divorce is established by marriage, not by personal freedom. Therefore, a slave husband, who is married, possesses this right.
This is the Hanafi position; other schools may differ but are not addressed here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أو حراً أو عبداً) للعمومات ولحديث ابن ماجه والدارقطني «الطلاق لمن أخذ بالساق»
His saying: (or a free man or a slave) due to general proofs and the hadith of Ibn Majah and al-Daraqutni: 'Divorce belongs to the one who takes hold of the leg.'
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This is a concise ruling statement from the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq, supported by general evidences from Quran and Sunnah, and specifically by a hadith recorded by Ibn Majah and al-Daraqutni. The hadith 'al-talaqu liman akhadha bi al-saq' is understood to mean that divorce is in the hand of the husband, whether he is free or slave. The Hanafi school affirms that a slave husband has the capacity to divorce his wife, just as a free man does, because the right of divorce is established by marriage and is not dependent on freedom. This is the majority view.

Key Terms
أخذ بالساق (akhadha bi al-saq): Idiom meaning 'took hold of the leg', referring to the husband who has authority over the wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Consensus of the companions
B) A hadith: 'Divorce belongs to the one who takes hold of the leg'
C) Analogy to marriage
D) Quranic verse

Key Takeaway
Divorce is valid from both free and slave husbands because the right to divorce arises from marriage, not from personal freedom.

The Invalidity of Divorce by a Minor or Insane Person
Ruling with proof from hadith and discussion on capacity and ratification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the divorce of a minor and an insane person, identify the proof from hadith, and explain why a minor cannot ratify it after puberty.

Core Idea
Divorce requires full legal capacity; the minor and insane lack it, so their divorce is invalid.

This passage clarifies a fundamental principle of Hanafi family law: divorce is not a neutral act but one that risks harm, requiring complete rational capacity. The matn states clearly that a minor (even if discerning) and an insane person cannot effect a valid divorce. The commentator provides the prophetic hadith as evidence, explores the nuance between validity and effectiveness, and addresses conflicting reports from early authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Subject
What is the act being ruled upon? Who are the agents excluded? Determine the hukm level (fard/wajib/haram/batil). The passage states 'no divorce of a minor or insane person' – the ruling is that such a divorce is invalid (batil).
The minor is defined as anyone who has not reached puberty, whether discerning or not.
2
Locate the Dalil
What proof does the author cite? The hadith: 'Every divorce is permissible except the divorce of a minor and an insane person.' The exception explicitly excludes these cases.
The hadith is ahad, so its thubut is zanni, but its dalala is considered qati' (clear) in this context.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? The word 'except' (illa) indicates a clear exception. The operative phrase 'except the divorce of a minor and an insane person' shows that their divorce is not permissible (ja'iz). The commentator notes that 'ja'iz' here is interpreted as 'effective' (nafidh) or better as 'valid' (sahih).
This distinction matters: a valid but non-effective divorce (like that of a fuduli) is covered if we take 'ja'iz' as 'sahih'.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf
Are there differing opinions? Reports from Ibn al-Musayyib, Ibn Umar, and Ibn Hanbal suggest a minor's divorce could be valid if he understands. The Hanafi response is to question the authenticity of these reports.
The commentator in Fath al-Qadir says 'Allah knows best about the authenticity of these transmissions.'
5
Evaluate the Hanafi Tarjih
Which position is relied upon? The Hanafi school upholds the clear hadith and the principle that a minor lacks capacity for harmful dispositions (like divorce). The mufta bihi is that a minor's divorce is invalid and cannot be ratified after puberty.
A minor's acceptance of Islam is valid because it is purely beneficial, unlike divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا طلاق الصبي والمجنون تصريح بما فهم سابقاً للحديث «كل طلاق جائز إلا طلاق الصبي والمجنون». والمراد بالجواز النفاذ كذا في فتح القدير والأولى أن يراد به الصحة ليدخل تحته طلاق الفضولي فإنه صحيح غير نافذ أطلق الصبي فشمل العاقل ولو مراهقاً لفقد أهلية التصرف خصوصاً ما. هو دائر بين النفع والضرر ونقل عن ابن المسيب وابن عمر رضي الله عنهم صحته منه ومثله عن ابن حنبل . قال في فتح القدير : والله أعلم بصحة هذه النقول. وإنما صح إسلامه لأنه حسن لذاته لا يقبل السقوط ونفع له. ولو طلق الصبي ثم بلغ فقال أجزت ذلك الطلاق لا يقع، ولو قال أوقعته وقع لأنه ابتداء إيقاع. كذا في الخانية . وفي البزازية : لو طلق رجل امرأة الصبي فلما بلغ الصبي قال أوقعت الطلاق الذي أوقعه فلان يقع، ولو قال أجزت ذلك لا يقع . وقال قبله : طلق النائم فلما انتبه قال لها طلقتك
His saying: (No divorce of a minor or an insane person) is a clarification of what was understood previously, due to the hadith: 'Every divorce is permissible except the divorce of a minor and an insane person.' By 'permissible' is meant effective, as in Fath al-Qadir, and it is better that it mean valid, so that it includes the divorce of a mediator, which is valid but not effective. He mentioned the minor absolutely, including the discerning one, even if he is an adolescent, due to his lack of capacity for disposition, especially regarding what involves benefit and harm. It is transmitted from Ibn al-Musayyib and Ibn Umar, may Allah be pleased with them, that it is valid from him, and similarly from Ibn Hanbal. He said in Fath al-Qadir: And Allah knows best the authenticity of these transmissions. His acceptance of Islam is valid because it is good in itself, does not accept abrogation, and benefits him. If a minor divorces, then reaches puberty and says 'I accept that divorce,' it does not occur. If he says 'I effect it,' it occurs because it is an initiation of divorce. Thus in al-Khaniyya. And in al-Bazzaziyya: If a man divorces the wife of a minor, then when the minor reaches puberty, he says 'I effect the divorce that so-and-so effected,' it occurs. If he says 'I accept that,' it does not occur. And he said before: A sleeping person divorced, then when he woke up he said to her 'You are divorced'.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof. The matn states that the divorce of a minor (sabi) and an insane person (majnun) is invalid. The commentator explains that the hadith 'Every divorce is permissible except the divorce of a minor and an insane person' is the evidence. There is a nuance: 'allowed' (ja'iz) here means effective (nafidh) according to Fath al-Qadir, but it is better interpreted as valid (sahih) so that it includes the divorce of a mediator (fuduli), which is valid but not effective until ratified. The minor is mentioned inclusively, covering even a discerning adolescent, because he lacks full legal capacity (ahliyyat al-tasarruf) for acts that involve potential harm (like divorce). There is a reported transmission from Ibn al-Musayyib, Ibn Umar, and Ibn Hanbal that a minor's divorce is valid, but the commentator expresses doubt about the authenticity of these reports. The reason a minor's acceptance of Islam is valid (unlike divorce) is that Islam is purely beneficial and does not involve harm. The passage then gives scenarios: a minor divorces and later ratifies it after puberty—the ratification does not make the original divorce effective; instead, if he says 'I effect it now,' it is a new divorce. Similarly, if someone else divorces the minor's wife, the minor can effect it after puberty but cannot simply accept it. The passage ends with an analogy to a sleeping person's divorce.

Key Terms
الصبي (al-sabi): A male child who has not reached puberty, even if discerning (mumayyiz).
المجنون (al-majnun): An insane person who lacks rational capacity.
النفاذ (al-nafadh): Effectiveness, the legal consequence of the divorce taking effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A 15-year-old boy (who has not reached puberty) divorces his wife. After he reaches puberty, he says 'I accept that divorce.' What is the ruling?
A) The divorce becomes effective
B) The divorce remains invalid
C) It is valid only if he said 'I effect it'
D) It is valid because he is now an adult

Key Takeaway
A minor's divorce is invalid and cannot be ratified after puberty; a new divorce must be initiated.

Divorce by a Sleeping Person: Intention Required
Ruling on divorce by a sleeping person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce by a sleeping person and distinguish the legal effect of different waking confirmations (e.g., 'I permitted' vs. 'I effected').

Core Idea
Divorce requires intention; a sleeping person's words have no legal effect.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a critical nuance in Hanafi divorce law: what happens when a husband speaks during sleep and later confirms his words while awake? The answer hinges on the precise wording he uses—whether he merely 'permits' or actively 'effects' the divorce. The ruling protects wives from unintended divorces while holding husbands accountable for deliberate confirmations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is pronouncing divorce while asleep. The ruling: it does not occur (لا يقع). Note that the sleeping person lacks intention, which is a condition for a valid divorce.
2
Distinguish post-waking utterances
After waking, the husband may say different phrases. 'I permitted that divorce' (أجزت) = no effect (mere consent). 'I effected that divorce' (أوقعت) = divorce occurs (references the concept, not the invalid utterance). 'I effected what I uttered' (أوقعت الذي تلفظت) = no effect (references the specific invalid speech).
The key is whether the wording points to the specific sleep-talk or to the general idea of divorce.
3
Trace the analogy to the minor
The same distinction applies to a minor who attains puberty: his earlier divorce pronouncements are invalid, and similar waking confirmations follow the same rules.
4
Examine the qiyas with the 'thousand times' case
The author analogizes to a man who says 'You are divorced a thousand times' then 'three times upon you and the rest upon her co-wives.' The excess over three is ineffective, just as the specific sleep-talk utterance is ineffective.
The 'illa (effective cause) is that the original utterance (sleep-talk or excess) was legally ineffective from the start.
5
Conclude the operative ruling
The mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is that divorce by a sleeping person does not occur. A waking confirmation only takes effect if the wording clearly indicates effecting a new divorce, not merely permitting or referencing the earlier invalid speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في النوم لا يقع، وكذا لو قال أجزت ذلك الطلاق، ولو قال أوقعت ذلك الطلاق يقع، ولو قال أوقعت الذي تلفظت به لا يقع، وكذا الصبي . والفرق أن قوله «أوقعت ذلك» يجوز أن يكون إشارة إلى الجنس، وقوله الذي تلفظت» إشارة إلى الشخص الذي حكم ببطلانه فأشبه ما إذا قال لها أنت طالق ألفاً ثم قال ثلاثاً عليك والباقي على ضراتها لأن الزائد على الثلاث غير عامل ا هـ . قوله : ( والنائم) أي لا يقع طلاق النائم، فلو قال لها بعدما استيقظ طلقتك في النوم أو أجزت ذلك الطلاق أو أوقعت ما تلفظت به حالة النوم لا يقع، ولو قال أوقعت ذلك الطلاق أو جعلته طلاقاً وقع وفيه من البحث ما قدمناه في طلاق الصبي.
Divorce in sleep does not occur, and likewise if he said 'I permitted that divorce', but if he said 'I effected that divorce' it occurs, and if he said 'I effected what I uttered' it does not occur, and likewise the minor. The difference is that his statement 'I effected that' may indicate the [general] species, while his statement 'what I uttered' indicates the specific [instance] which was ruled invalid, so it is similar to when he says to her 'You are divorced a thousand times' then says 'three times upon you and the rest upon her co-wives' because the excess over three is ineffective. End. His statement: (And the sleeping person) means the divorce of a sleeping person does not occur. If he said to her after waking up 'I divorced you in sleep' or 'I permitted that divorce' or 'I effected what I uttered while asleep' it does not occur. And if he said 'I effected that divorce' or 'I made it a divorce' it occurs, and in it is the investigation we previously presented in the divorce of a minor.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the divorce of a sleeping person, presented as a commentary on the matn phrase 'والنائم'. The author first states the general ruling: divorce pronounced while asleep does not occur. He then examines different formulations a husband might use after waking up concerning his sleep-talk. If he says 'I permitted that divorce' (أجزت ذلك الطلاق), this does not count as a divorce because 'permission' implies mere consent, not an actual utterance of the divorce formula. Similarly, if he says 'I effected what I uttered' (أوقعت الذي تلفظت به), referring specifically to the words he spoke in sleep, it does not occur because those words were invalid. However, if he says 'I effected that divorce' (أوقعت ذلك الطلاق) or 'I made it a divorce' (جعلته طلاقاً), the divorce occurs. The difference is that 'that' (ذلك) can refer to the general concept of divorce, while 'what I uttered' refers to the particular invalid utterance. The author analogizes this to the case of a man who says 'You are divorced a thousand times' and then says 'three times upon you and the rest upon her co-wives' – the excess over three is ineffective. The same reasoning applies to the minor: his divorce does not occur, and similar phrases after attaining maturity have the same distinctions.

Key Terms
النائم (al-na'im): The sleeping person; one who is asleep and not conscious.
الصبي (al-sabi): The minor child who has not reached puberty.
أجزت (ajaztu): I permitted; I allowed. In the context, it indicates mere consent, not an active effecting.
أوقعت (awaqatu): I effected; I caused to fall. It actively brings about the divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following waking confirmations does NOT make the divorce occur?
A) 'I effected that divorce'
B) 'I made it a divorce'
C) 'I permitted that divorce'
D) 'I effected that divorce' (same as A)

Key Takeaway
Divorce by a sleeping person is invalid; only a waking confirmation that actively references the general concept of divorce (not the specific invalid utterance) can effect a new divorce.

Mental Incapacity and its Variants in Divorce
Definition of mental incapacity conditions and their effect on divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the four types of mental incapacity (al-majnun, al-ma'tuh, al-mubarsam, al-madhush) and state the Hanafi ruling on divorce for each.

Core Idea
Divorce requires intention; mental incapacity invalidates it, but subtle distinctions matter for barasm and shock.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines several conditions of mental incapacity and explains how they affect the validity of divorce. Understanding these terms is crucial because not every mental disturbance invalidates talaq. The text distinguishes between types and provides detailed case rulings from the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Define the four conditions
Identify the four terms: al-majnun (insane), al-ma'tuh (mentally deficient), al-mubarsam (barasm-afflicted), al-madhush (shocked). Note the distinctions: the ma'tuh is not physically punished; the mubarsam has a specific medical condition; the madhush has lost his mind due to shock/sorrow.
All four involve some degree of loss of intellect.
2
Determine the legal effect on divorce
The Hanafi ruling is that divorce by a person who has lost his mind (any of these conditions) is invalid (batil). This is because talaq requires intention and understanding.
Only simple bewilderment (tahayyur) does not invalidate divorce.
3
Examine the tafwid case and Muhammad's principle
When a husband delegates the right to divorce (tafwid) and then becomes insane, Muhammad distinguishes between revocable and irrevocable speech. If the delegation is revocable, insanity nullifies it; if irrevocable, it remains.
This principle is specific to the case of tafwid.
4
Consider the wakalah (agency) case
Agency is voided if the principal becomes insane for a long period, but not if momentary. Abu Hanifa did not fix a specific duration.
This shows nuance in defining 'long' vs 'short' periods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأراد بالمجنون من في عقله اختلال فيدخل المعتوه، وأحسن الأقوال في الفرق بينهما أن المعتوه هو القليل الفهم المختلط الكلام الفاسد التدبير لكن لا يضرب ولا يشتم بخلاف المجنون، ويدخل المبرسم والمغمى عليه والمدهوش. وفي الصحاح : البرسام داء معروف. وفي بعض كتب الطب إنه ورم حار يعرض للحجاب الذي بين الكبد والمعالم يتصل بالدماغ، وهو معرب وبرسم الرجل بالبناء للمفعول يقال برسام وبلسام وهو مبرسم ومبلسم ا هـ . وفي الخانية: رجل عرف أنه كان مجنوناً فقالت له امرأته طلقني البارحة فقال أصابني الجنون ولا يعرف ذلك إلا بقوله كان القول قوله . ثم قال : رجل طلق امرأته وهو فلما صاحب برسم صح قال قد طلقت امرأتي ثم قال إني كنت أظن أن الطلاق في تلك الحالة لا يقع كان واقعاً. قال مشايخنا رحمهم الله تعالى حين ما أقر بالطلاق إن رده إلى حالة البرسام بأن قال قد طلقت امرأتي حالة البراسم فالطلاق غير واقع، وإن لم يرده إلى حالة البرسام فهو مأخوذ بذلك قضاء. وقال الفقيه أبو الليث هذا إذا لم يكن إقراره بذلك في حالة مذاكرة الطلاق اهـ. وفيه أيضاً : لو قال لامرأته طلقي نفسك إذا شئت ثم جن الرجل جنوناً مطبقاً ثم طلقت المرأة نفسها قال محمد : كل شيء يملك الزوج أن يرجع عن كلامه يبطل بالجنون، وكل شيء لم يملك أن يرجع عن كلامه لا يبطل بالجنون. وفيها أيضاً : لو جن الموكل بطلت وكالته إن جن زماناً طويلاً، وإن كان ساعة لا تبطل ولم يوقت أو حنيفة فيه شيأ ا هـ . قوله (والمدهوش) قال الرملي في حواشي المنح : المراد بالمدهوش من ذهب عقله من ذهل أو وله لا مطلق المتحير، وهذا الذي يجب أن يفسر به إذ التحير لا يمنع وقوع الطلاق وقد قال في القاموس : دهش كفرح فهو دهش تحير أو ذهب عقله من ذهل أو وله والذاهل المتحير والوله محركة الحزن أو ذهاب العقل خوفاً والحيرة والخوف فرجع المعنى في كلامهم أو ذهب عقله من التحير والخوف فيكون نوعاً من الجنون . اه ملخصاً وكلام المؤلف ظاهر في ذلك
And by 'the insane' is meant one whose intellect has disorder, so it includes the mentally deficient. The best statements on the difference between them is that the mentally deficient is one of little understanding, confused speech, corrupt management, but he is neither beaten nor reviled, unlike the insane. And it includes the barasm-afflicted, the unconscious, and the shocked. In Al-Sihah: 'Barasm is a known disease.' And in some books of medicine: 'It is a hot tumor that occurs in the diaphragm between the liver and the stomach, connecting to the brain.' It is an Arabized word. A man is said to be 'burisma' (passive) and is called 'birsam' and 'balsam', and he is 'mubarsam' and 'mubalsam'. End. And in Al-Khaniyya: 'A man known to have been insane; his wife said to him "Divorce me last night," and he said "Insanity befell me." This is not known except by his statement; his word is accepted.' Then he said: 'A man divorced his wife while he was... Then when he had a fit of barasm and recovered, he said "I divorced my wife," then said "I thought that divorce in that state would not occur" – it is effective.' Our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) said: 'When he acknowledges the divorce, if he refers it to the state of barasm (by saying "I divorced my wife in the state of barasm"), the divorce is not effective. If he does not refer it to the state of barasm, he is held to it judicially.' Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: 'This is if his acknowledgment was not in the context of discussing divorce.' End. And in it also: 'If he said to his wife "Divorce yourself when you wish," then the man went insane with continuous madness, then the woman divorced herself, Muhammad said: Everything that the husband has the power to revoke from his speech is nullified by insanity, and everything he does not have the power to revoke from his speech is not nullified by insanity.' And in it also: 'If the principal (muwakkil) becomes insane, his agency is void if he is insane for a long period; if it is for a moment, it is not void, and Abu Hanifa did not set a time limit.' End. His statement (and the shocked): Al-Ramli in Hawashi al-Minah said: 'By "al-madhush" is meant one whose mind has gone due to shock or intense sorrow, not simply the bewildered person. This is what must be interpreted, since bewilderment does not prevent divorce from occurring.' And in Al-Qamus: 'Dahasha (like faraha) he is dahish: bewildered or his mind went due to shock or intense sorrow. Al-dhahil is bewildered, and al-walah (with fatha) is grief or loss of mind from fear. Bewilderment and fear.' So the meaning in their speech is 'or his mind went from bewilderment and fear, so it becomes a type of insanity.' End, summarized. And the author's words are evident in that.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling concerning mental incapacity conditions and their impact on divorce. The author begins by defining 'the insane' (المجنون) as one whose intellect is disturbed. This includes the mentally deficient (المعتوه), who is distinguished by having little understanding and confused speech but is not physically punished, unlike the insane. Also included are those afflicted with barasm (a disease affecting the brain-liver connection), the unconscious, and the shocked (المدهوش). The text then cites from Al-Khaniyya several cases: a man claims insanity at the time of divorce; if he acknowledges the divorce but refers it to the state of barasm, it is not effective; otherwise, he is held to it. Another case: a husband gives his wife the option to divorce herself, then becomes continuously insane; she divorces herself. Imam Muhammad states a general principle: anything the husband can revoke is nullified by insanity; anything he cannot revoke remains. This applies to options that are revocable. Additionally, the agency (wakalah) of a principal is void if he becomes insane for a long time, but not if momentary; Abu Hanifa did not fix a specific duration. Finally, the term 'al-madhush' (the shocked) is defined: it is not mere bewilderment but actual loss of mind from shock or intense sorrow, making it a type of insanity. The author's commentary aligns with this.

Key Terms
المجنون (al-majnun): One whose intellect is disturbed; insane.
المعتوه (al-ma'tuh): The mentally deficient: little understanding, confused speech, corrupt management, but not beaten or reviled.
المبرسم (al-mubarsam): One afflicted with barasm, a hot tumor in the diaphragm connecting to the brain.
المدهوش (al-madhush): One whose mind has gone due to shock or intense sorrow, not mere bewilderment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which condition does NOT prevent divorce from occurring?
A) Continuous insanity (al-junun al-mutbaq)
B) Barasm (al-mubarsam)
C) Simple bewilderment (mujarrad al-tahayyur)
D) Being unconscious (al-mughma 'alayh)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that loss of mind from any cause (insanity, mental deficiency, barasm, shock) invalidates divorce; mere bewilderment does not.

Master's Inability to Divorce His Slave's Wife
Ruling that master's divorce is ineffective

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a master's divorce pronouncement on his slave's wife is invalid and explain the reason based on the principle that the master is not a party to the marriage contract.

Core Idea
A master cannot divorce his slave's wife because he is not a party to the marriage.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in Hanafi fiqh: the master's relation to his slave's marriage. While the master owns the slave, he does not own the marriage bond. Therefore, any divorce pronouncement by the master on the slave's wife is ineffective. We will explore the legal basis and practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
Recognize the master, the slave, and the slave's wife. The master pronounces divorce on the wife. Determine who has authority over the marriage.
The master is not the husband; only the husband or his delegate can divorce.
2
Apply the principle of marriage authority
In Hanafi fiqh, divorce is the right of the husband or his authorized agent. The master has no inherent authority over the slave's marriage contract.
Ownership of the slave does not extend to the marriage bond.
3
State the ruling
The master's divorce pronouncement is batil (invalid) and does not produce any legal effect.
This is settled in Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والسيد على امرأة عبده أي لا يقع لما روينا وفي الخانية من فصل النكاح على الشرط المولى : إذا زوج أمته من عبده إن بدأ العبد فقال زوجني أمتك هذه على أن أمرها بيدك تطلقها كما شئت فزوجها منه يجوز النكاح ولا يكون الأمر بيد المولى، ولو ابتدأ المولى فقال زوجتك أمتي على أن أمرها بيدي أطلقها كما أريد فقل العبد قبلت جاز النكاح ويكون الأمر بيد المولى ا هـ. فإن قلت ما الحيلة في صيرورة الأمر بيده من غير توقف على قبول العبد فإن في هذه الصورة قد تم النكاح بقول المولى زوجتك أمتي فيمكن العبد أن لا يقبل فلا مر بيد المولى؟ قلت يمتنع المولى من تزويجه حتى يقول العبد قبل التزويج إذا تزوجتها فأمرها بيدك أبداً ثم يزوجها المولى له فيكون الأمر بيد المولى ولا يمكنه إخراجه أبداً، والفرع مذكور في الخانية أيضاً في ذلك الفصل
His statement: (And the master over his slave's wife) means it does not occur, based on what we have narrated. And in Al-Khaniyya, from the chapter of marriage on condition: the master: If he marries his female slave to his slave, if the slave initiates by saying 'Marry me to your female slave on condition that her affair is in your hand, you divorce her as you wish,' and he marries her to him, the marriage is valid and the affair is not in the master's hand. And if the master initiates and says 'I marry you to my female slave on condition that her affair is in my hand, I divorce her as I wish,' and the slave says 'I accept,' the marriage is valid and the affair is in the master's hand. End. If you say: What is the legal trick (hila) for the affair to be in his hand without depending on the slave's acceptance? For in this scenario the marriage is concluded by the master's statement 'I marry you to my female slave,' and the slave may not accept, so the affair is not in the master's hand? I say: The master refrains from marrying him until the slave says before marriage: 'If I marry her, her affair is in your hand forever.' Then the master marries her to him, so the affair is in the master's hand and he can never remove it. This branch is also mentioned in Al-Khaniyya in that chapter.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling that a master's pronouncement of divorce on his slave's wife is not effective. The master has no direct authority over the marriage bond; it belongs to the slave husband. However, the master can arrange to retain authority over divorce by stipulating in the marriage contract. The text presents two scenarios from Al-Khaniyya. In the first, the slave initiates by asking to marry the master's female slave on condition that the master has authority to divorce. In this case, the marriage is valid but the authority does not belong to the master because the condition was proposed by the slave and not inherent in the contract. In the second, the master initiates by saying 'I marry you to my female slave on condition that her affair is in my hand,' and the slave accepts. Here the authority belongs to the master. The text then addresses a potential problem: if the master initiates, the slave may not accept the condition, and the master loses authority. The solution (hila) is for the master to withhold the marriage until the slave voluntarily declares before the marriage that if he marries, the affair will be in the master's hand forever. Then the master marries him, and the condition is binding. This ensures the master's authority without relying on the slave's acceptance after the contract.

Key Terms
السيد (al-sayyid): The master who owns a slave.
الحيلة (al-hila): A legal trick or stratagem to achieve a lawful outcome within the framework of the Shariah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master says to his slave's wife 'You are divorced,' what is the ruling?
A) The divorce occurs
B) The divorce does not occur
C) It is valid if the master intends it
D) It is a suspended divorce

Key Takeaway
The master's divorce on his slave's wife is invalid because the master is not a party to the marriage; only the husband or his delegate can divorce.

Legal Stratagem for Master's Authority over Slave's Divorce
Legal stratagem for master's authority over divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid legal stratagem (hila) for a master to retain authority over his slave's divorce, following the Hanafi method from Al-Khaniyya.

Core Idea
A master can secure divorce authority through a pre-marriage declaration by the slave, ensuring the condition is binding.

This lesson explores a hila (legal stratagem) from the Hanafi tradition that allows a master to retain the power of divorce over his slave's wife. The key is to have the slave voluntarily declare before marriage that the matter is in the master's hand. This ensures the master's authority without relying on acceptance after contract.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
A master's general divorce pronouncement on his slave's wife is ineffective. To gain authority, he must include a condition in the marriage contract.
The condition must be accepted by the slave.
2
Analyze the two scenarios from the text
First scenario: slave initiates condition → master does not get authority. Second scenario: master initiates condition and slave accepts → master gets authority. Identify the flaw: the slave may not accept.
The second scenario depends on the slave's consent.
3
Apply the hila (legal trick)
The master refrains from marrying until the slave voluntarily declares before marriage: 'If I marry her, her affair is in your hand forever.' Then the master marries him. The condition becomes binding and the master retains authority.
The declaration is made before the contract, so acceptance after is not needed.

His statement: (And the master over his slave's wife) means it does not occur, based on what we have narrated. And in Al-Khaniyya, from the chapter of marriage on condition: the master: If he marries his female slave to his slave, if the slave initiates by saying 'Marry me to your female slave on condition that her affair is in your hand, you divorce her as you wish,' and he marries her to him, the marriage is valid and the affair is not in the master's hand. The master has no direct authority over the marriage bond; it belongs to the slave husband. However, the master can arrange to retain authority over divorce by stipulating in the marriage contract.
