يقع إن كان يعرف نسبها وإن كان لا يعرف يقع ديانة وإن نوى امرأته في هذه الوجوه توسه ونوى الطلاق قال يقع قوله : ( وقيد بخطابها لأنه لو قال الخ اعترض عليه بأن عبارة البزازية لا تفيد أن عدم الوقوع لعدم الخطاب حتى يؤخذ منه فائدة التقييد بالخطاب في كلام المصنف . وأجيب بأن خصوص الخطاب ليس مراداً بل ما هو الأعم منه أو ما يقوم مقامه كالإضافة وذكر الاسم بدليل ما يأتي ا هـ . وهذا الجواب في نفسه حسن لكن يبعد أن يكون مراداً للمؤلف ما يأتي قبيل قول المتن ولو قال أنت الطلاق من قوله والحاصل أن قولهم الصريح لا يحتاج إلى النية إنما هو في القضاء، وأما في الديانة فمحتاج إليها لكن وقوعه في القضاء بلا نية إنما هو بشرط أن يقصدها بالخطاب الخ . هذا وفي القنية عن المحيط : رجل دعته جماعة إلى شرب الخمر فقال إني حلفت بالطلاق أني لا أشرب وكان كاذباً فيه ثم شرب طلقت. وقال صاحب التحفة : لا تطلق ديانة أي فقوله «طلقت أي قضاء وهو موافق لما مر من أنه إذا أقر بالطلاق كاذباً وقع قضاء لا ديانة، وظاهر أن قول البزازية هنا لا يقع أي قضاء ففيه مخالفة لهذا، وقد ذكر في لسان الحكام عبارة البزازية ثم أعقبها بعبارة القنية ولم يتعرض لهما. ويمكن أن يوفق بينهما بأن ما في البزازية محمول على إنشاء الحلف لا على الإخبار وما في القنية على الإخبار لقوله وكان كاذباً فيه لكن بعد هذا يرد على ما في القنية أن قوله إني حلفت بالطلاق» يحتمل الحلف بطلاق امرأة أخرى إلا أن يحمل على أنه ليس له امرأة غيرها فيكون إخباراً عن طلاق مضاف إليها. وما في البزازية محمول على أن له غيرها وإلا لا يصدق بدليل ما يأتي عن الظهيرية من قوله : لو قال امرأته طالق ولم يسم وله امرأة معروفة طلقت استحساناً، وإن قال لي امرأة أخرى وإياها عنيت لا يقبل قوله إلا أن يقيم البينة. هذا اهـ. ما ظهر لي فتأمل وراجع. طلقت قضاء وديانة
As I consider her divorced as in al-Khaniyya. And it is restricted by his addressing her because if he said 'I swore by divorce' and did not add [an address] to her, it does not occur [as in] al-Bazzaziyya from the section on oaths: its expression: he said to her 'Do not leave the house except with my permission, for I have sworn by divorce,' and she left, it does not occur due to the absence of mentioning his oath by her divorce, and it is possible [he meant] swearing by divorce of someone else, so the saying is his. And mentioning her name or adding it to him is like addressing her as we explained. So if he said 'Divorced' and it was said to him 'Whom did you intend?' and he said 'My wife,' his wife is divorced. And if he said 'A woman is divorced' or said 'I divorced a woman three times' and said 'I did not mean my wife by it,' he is believed. And if he said 'Amrah is divorced' and his wife is Amrah and he said 'I did not mean my wife by it,' his wife is divorced and he is not believed in court. Similarly, if he said 'So-and-so's daughter is divorced' mentioning the father's name without mentioning the woman's name and his wife is so-and-so's daughter and he said 'I did not mean my wife,' he is not believed in court and his wife is divorced. Similarly, if he did not attribute her to her father but rather attributed her to her mother or her child, she is divorced — as in al-Khaniyya, and in Fath al-Qadir he added: or attributed her to her sister. And in another place of it: a man said 'My wife Amrah bint Subayh is divorced' and his wife is Amrah bint Hafs and he had no intention, his wife is not divorced. And if Subayh was the husband of his wife's mother and she was attributed to him while in his guardianship, and he said that, whether he knows his wife's lineage or not, his wife is divorced and he is not believed in court; and between him and Allah it does not occur if he knows her lineage, and if he does not know it occurs according to religious duty. And if he intended his wife in these cases... and intended divorce, he said it occurs. His statement: (And it is restricted by his addressing her because if he said... etc.) objection was raised against it that the expression of al-Bazzaziyya does not indicate that non-occurrence is due to absence of address such that the benefit of restriction by address in the author's words would be taken. And it was answered that specific address is not intended, rather what is more general than it or what stands in its place like addition and mentioning the name, as indicated by what follows. This answer is good in itself but it is unlikely that the author intended what follows shortly before the text's statement 'And if he said 'You are the divorce'... from his statement: 'The gist is that their statement 'express [divorce] does not need intention' is only in court, as for religious duty it is needed but its occurrence in court without intention is only on condition that he intended her by the address...' This and in al-Qunyah from al-Muhit: a man invited by a group to drink wine said 'I have sworn by divorce that I will not drink' and he was lying in it, then he drank, she is divorced. And the author of al-Tuhfah said: she is not divorced according to religious duty, meaning his statement 'divorced' means in court, and it agrees with what passed that if he admitted to divorce falsely, it occurs in court not in religious duty. And it is apparent that the statement of al-Bazzaziyya here 'it does not occur' means in court, so there is conflict with this. And it was mentioned in Lisan al-Hukkam the expression of al-Bazzaziyya then followed it with the expression of al-Qunyah and did not address them. And it is possible to reconcile them by saying that what is in al-Bazzaziyya is interpreted as initiating the oath, not as reporting, and what is in al-Qunyah as reporting because of his statement 'and he was lying in it.' But after this, an objection arises against what is in al-Qunyah that his statement 'I have sworn by divorce' could be swearing by divorce of another woman unless it is interpreted that he has no other wife, so it is a report of divorce attributed to her. And what is in al-Bazzaziyya is interpreted that he has other wives, otherwise he would not be believed, as indicated by what follows from al-Dhahiriyyah from his statement: if he said 'my wife is divorced' and did not name, and he has a known wife, she is divorced by istihsan, and if he said 'I have another wife and I intended her', his statement is not accepted unless he brings proof. This, the end. What appeared to me, so contemplate and refer. She is divorced in court and religious duty.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq dealing with the ruling when a husband says 'I swore by divorce' (حلفت بالطلاق) without specifying his wife. The author clarifies that the ruling is restricted to cases where the husband addresses his wife directly or mentions her name or adds her to himself, because otherwise the divorce does not occur. The passage includes many sub-rulings: if he says 'Divorced' and is asked whom he meant, he is believed if he says his wife; if he says 'A woman is divorced' and claims he meant another, he is believed; but if he names a woman who happens to be his wife, he is not believed in court even if he claims he meant another. The text also discusses a lying scenario: a man falsely swears by divorce that he will not drink wine, then drinks. According to al-Qunyah, the divorce occurs in court but not religiously according to al-Tuhfah. The author reconciles the apparent conflict by distinguishing between initiating an oath (إنشاء) and reporting an oath (إخبار). In the initiating case, the divorce does not occur even in court because the oath is not addressed to the wife; in the reporting case, if the man admits he lied, the divorce occurs in court but not religiously. The passage emphasizes the importance of intention and the distinction between qada' (court ruling) and diyanah (religious duty).

Key Terms
حلفت بالطلاق (halaftu bi al-talaq): Saying 'I swore by divorce' as an oath; if the husband says this without adding his wife's name, no divorce occurs unless he intends her.
قضاء وديانة (qada'an wa diyanatan): Court ruling and religious duty; a divorce may occur in court (qada) but not in religious duty (diyanah) if the husband is lying, and vice versa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Divorce occurs only if he repeated it three times.

Key Takeaway
Saying 'I swore by divorce' does not divorce unless the wife is clearly addressed, named, or added to the husband; ambiguous oaths are not counted as divorce in court.

Lying About Swearing by Divorce: Distinguishing Initiation from Report
Resolves the conflict between al-Bazzaziyyah and al-Qunyah on lying oaths of divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the distinction between initiating an oath (insha) and reporting an oath (ikhbar) to cases where a husband lies about swearing by divorce, and differentiate between qada and diyanah rulings.

Core Idea
A lying oath of divorce: if initiated, no divorce; if reported, divorce in court but not religiously.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced scenario: a husband falsely claims he swore by divorce that he would not drink wine, then drinks. Two Hanafi sources, al-Bazzaziyyah and al-Qunyah, appear to conflict on whether divorce occurs. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq reconciles them by distinguishing between initiating an oath (إنشاء) and reporting an oath (إخبار). When the man is lying, if he is initiating a new oath, no divorce occurs at all; if he is reporting a past oath (i.e., admitting he previously swore by divorce and is now breaking it), then divorce occurs in court (qada) but not in religious duty (diyanah). This lesson solidifies the critically important fiqh distinction between qada (court ruling) and diyanah (religious obligation).

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting reports
Al-Bazzaziyyah states that if a man says 'I swore by divorce that I would not drink' and then drinks, no divorce occurs. Al-Qunyah states that if he is lying, divorce occurs. These appear contradictory.
The conflict is intra-Hanafi.
2
Understand the reconciliation
The author reconciles by interpreting al-Bazzaziyyah's case as initiating an oath (insha), where the man is creating a new oath. Al-Qunyah's case is reporting a past oath (ikhbar), where the man admits he previously swore and is now lying about it.
In the ikhbar case, the man's admission is treated as an iqrar (confession) of a past divorce oath.
3
Apply the qada vs diyanah distinction
In the ikhbar case, since the man admits he lied, the court rules divorce occurs (qada), but religiously, no divorce occurs because he did not actually swear (diyanah). In the insha case, no divorce even in court.
The key is the man's admission of lying triggers the court ruling.
4
Consider additional nuances
If the man has no other wife, the statement is automatically a report about his wife. If he has other wives, he could be swearing by divorce of another, so he is believed unless he has a known wife.
Refer to the rule from al-Dhahiriyyah: if he says 'my wife' and has a known wife, she is divorced by istihsan.

This, the end. What appeared to me, so contemplate and refer. She is divorced in court and religious duty.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq dealing with the ruling when a husband says 'I swore by divorce' (حلفت بالطلاق) without specifying his wife. The author clarifies that the ruling is restricted to cases where the husband addresses his wife directly or mentions her name or adds her to himself, because otherwise the divorce does not occur. The passage includes many sub-rulings: if he says 'Divorced' and is asked whom he meant, he is believed if he says his wife; if he says 'A woman is divorced' and claims he meant another, he is believed; but if he names a woman who happens to be his wife, he is not believed in court even if he claims he meant another. The text also discusses a lying scenario: a man falsely swears by divorce that he will not drink wine, then drinks. According to al-Qunyah, the divorce occurs in court but not religiously according to al-Tuhfah. The author reconciles the apparent conflict by distinguishing between initiating an oath (إنشاء) and reporting an oath (إخبار). In the initiating case, the divorce does not occur even in court because the oath is not addressed to the wife; in the reporting case, if the man admits he lied, the divorce occurs in court but not religiously. The passage emphasizes the importance of intention and the distinction between qada' (court ruling) and diyanah (religious duty).

Key Terms
حلفت بالطلاق (halaftu bi al-talaq): Saying 'I swore by divorce' as an oath; if the husband says this without adding his wife's name, no divorce occurs unless he intends her.
قضاء وديانة (qada'an wa diyanatan): Court ruling and religious duty; a divorce may occur in court (qada) but not in religious duty (diyanah) if the husband is lying, and vice versa.
إنشاء وإخبار (insha' wa ikhbar): Initiating an oath vs reporting an oath; the reconciliation between al-Bazzaziyyah and al-Qunyah hinges on this distinction.

Ruling on False Description of Wife in Divorce Pronouncement
Explains that false descriptions do not affect divorce if identification is clear.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that identification by relation (nisbah) or pointing (isharah) takes precedence over false descriptions to determine the validity of a divorce pronouncement.

Core Idea
False description does not invalidate divorce if the wife is identified by correct relation or pointing.

When a husband pronounces divorce but adds an incorrect attribute—like calling a sighted wife 'blind'—does the divorce take effect? The Hanafi school distinguishes between different methods of identification. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that the key is whether the wife is sufficiently identified; a false description is ignored if the identification is clear by relation to her father or by pointing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the identification method
Determine whether the husband identified the wife by relation (e.g., 'Bint so-and-so'), by pointing (e.g., 'this one'), or only by a name/description without these.
Relation and pointing are strong means; mere description or false name without them is weak.
2
Check if the relation or pointing is correct
If the relation (father's name) is correct, or if he pointed to his wife, the identification is valid even if a description is false.
A false name (e.g., calling her 'Amrah' when her name is 'Fatima') without pointing fails.
3
Apply the rule: false description is void
Once identification by relation is established, any additional false description is legally irrelevant—the divorce occurs.
Example: 'Bint Subayh, who has a mole,' when there is no mole—the mole is ignored.
4
Consider the effect of pointing
If the husband points, the pointing overrides both naming and description. The pointed woman is divorced even if the name or description refers to someone else.
Pointing is the clearest identification.
5
Derive the principle
The overarching principle is that the objective is clear identification of the wife. False attributes do not defeat a correct identification.
This prevents the husband from later claiming he meant a different woman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال امرأته الحبشية طالق وامرأته ليست بحبشية لا يقع، ولو كان له امرأة بصيرة فقال امرأته هذه العمياء ،طالق وأشار إلى البصيرة تطلق البصيرة ولا تعتبر التسمية ولا الصفة. مع الإشارة ا هـ . وفي المحيط الأصل أنه متى وجدت النسبة وغير اسمها بغيره لا يقع لأن التعريف لا يحصل بالتسمية متى بدل اسمها لأن بذلك الاسم تكون امرأة أجنبية، ولو بدل اسمها وأشار إليها يقع ثم قال : ولو قال امرأتي بنت صبيح أو بنت فلان التي في وجهها خال طالق ولم يكن لها خال، وكذا التي هي عمياء أو زمنى وهي بصيرة صحيحة طالق طلقت، وذكر العمى والزمن باطل لأنه عرف امرأته بالنسبة ووصفها بصفة فصح التعريف ولغت الصفة. ولو قال امرأتي عمرة أم ولدي هذه الجالسة طالق ولا نية له والجالسة غيرها وليست بامرأته لم تطلق لأنه سماها وأشار والعبرة للإشارة لا للتسمية ا.هـ.
And if he said 'My Abyssinian wife is divorced' and his wife is not Abyssinian, it does not occur. And if he had a sighted wife and said 'My wife, this blind one, is divorced' and pointed to the sighted one, the sighted one is divorced and the naming and description are not considered with the pointing. And in al-Muhit: the principle is that whenever relation is found and he changes her name to something else, it does not occur because identification is not achieved by naming when he changes her name, because by that name she is an alien woman. And if he changes her name and points to her, it occurs. Then he said: And if he said 'My wife Bint Subayh or Bint so-and-so who has a mole on her face is divorced' and she did not have a mole, similarly if she is blind or paralyzed while she is sighted and healthy, [and he says] 'divorced', she is divorced. And mentioning blindness and paralysis is void because he identified his wife by relation and described her with a description, so identification is correct and the description is void. And if he said 'My wife Amrah, mother of my child, this sitting one, is divorced' and he has no intention, and the sitting one is someone else and not his wife, she is not divorced because he named and pointed, and the criterion is the pointing, not the naming.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage discusses cases where a husband pronounces divorce but describes his wife with an incorrect attribute. The general rule from al-Muhit: if the husband identifies his wife by a relation (e.g., by her father's name), even if he adds a false description, the divorce takes effect because the identification is clear and the description is ignored. However, if he uses only a name that is not his wife's, the divorce does not occur unless he points to her. For example, if he says 'My Abyssinian wife' and she is not Abyssinian, no divorce. But if he says 'Bint Subayh' and that is his wife's father, even if he adds a false mole, the divorce occurs. The key is the means of identification: relation (نسبة) takes precedence over description. If he points to her, the pointing overrides any false naming or description.

Key Terms
النسبة (al-nisbah): Attribution to a person, e.g., saying 'Bint so-and-so' (daughter of so-and-so) to identify the wife.
الإشارة (al-isharah): Pointing to the wife; if the husband points, that takes precedence over naming and description.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The divorce only occurs if he points to her
D) The divorce is suspended until he clarifies

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a false description of the wife does not invalidate a divorce if the wife is identified by a correct relation or by pointing.

Effect of 'Then' and 'And' in Multiple Divorces
Explains the legal effect of conjunctions in multiple divorces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the effect of 'then' (ثم) versus 'and' (و) when pronouncing divorce on multiple wives and apply the rule to various scenarios.

Core Idea
'Then' separates divorces; 'and' joins them — the conjunction determines who is divorced.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a key linguistic nuance in Hanafi fiqh: the conjunctions used in divorce pronouncements can drastically change the outcome. Understanding the difference between 'then' (ثم) and 'and' (و) is essential for properly applying talaq rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule for 'then' and 'and'
Based on the passage, identify the core function of each conjunction: 'then' cuts off connection, 'and' joins.
The text says: 'Because the word 'then' cuts off connection' and 'and' is for connecting and joining.
2
Analyze the four-wife example
Consider the scenario: a man says to his four wives 'You, then you, then you, then you are divorced.' Determine which wife is divorced and explain why.
The text states only the fourth is divorced because 'then' interrupts the connection.
3
Examine the subsidiary cases
Work through the variations: 'one and one', 'you and you', 'you and you two', etc. For each, note the ruling and the underlying principle.
Pay attention to the role of intention where mentioned.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi divergence
Compare the ruling in al-Dhahiriyyah with the variation in al-Fatawa regarding 'You are divorced one and one'. Identify the similarity and any difference in other cases.
Both sources agree on 'one and one' giving one divorce, but intention may affect other scenarios.
5
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Given the general rule, predict the outcome for a hypothetical case not explicitly mentioned, such as using 'then' for some wives and 'and' for others.
Use the principle that 'then' creates separate blocks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنه في موضع آخر : رجل له أربع نسوة فقال أنت ثم أنت ثم أنت ثم أنت طالق طلقت الرابعة لا غير لأنه ما أوصل الإيقاع إلا بالرابعة لأن كلمة «ثم تقطع الوصل ا.هـ. وهو يفيد أنه لو كان بالواو وقع على الكل لأنها للوصل الجمع. وصرح في الظهيرية بأن الواو كذلك وعبارتها : ولو قال أنت طالق واحدة وواحدة تقع واحدة ولو قال أنت طالق وأنت يقع ثنتان. وفي الفتاوى : واحدة. ولو قال وأنت لامرأة أخرى يقع عليها، ولو قال أنت طالق وأنتما للأولى والثانية يقع على الأولى ثنتان وعلى الثانية واحدة. ولو قال أنت طالق أولاً بل أنت يقع واحدة، ولو قال ثانياً أنت للأخرى لا يقع بدون النية، فأما وأنت تقع واحدة كقوله هذه طالق وهذه يقع عليها، ولو قال هذه وهذه طالق طلقتا، ولو قال هذه هذه طالق لم تطلق الأولى إلا أن يقول طالقان، ولو قال هذه طالق هذه لم يقع على الأخرى بدون النية، ولو قال لهن أنت ثم أنت ثم أنت طالق طلقت الأخيرة، وكذا بحرف الواو . ولو قال طوالق طلقن، ولو قدم الطلاق طلقن، ولو قال هذه طالق معك لم يقع على المخاطبة إلا بالنية ا هـ . وسيأتي ما إذا نادى امرأته فأجابه غيرها.
And from it in another place: a man had four wives and said 'You, then you, then you, then you are divorced' — only the fourth is divorced because he did not effect the pronouncement except upon the fourth, since the word 'then' cuts off connection. This indicates that if it were with 'and', it would occur upon all because it is for connecting and joining. And it is explicitly stated in al-Dhahiriyyah that 'and' is likewise: its expression: if he said 'You are divorced, one and one', one divorce occurs; if he said 'You are divorced and you', two divorces occur; and in al-Fatawa: one. And if he said 'and you' for another woman, it occurs upon her; if he said 'You are divorced and you two' for the first and second, upon the first two divorces and upon the second one. And if he said 'You are divorced, first, but you', one divorce occurs; if he said 'second, you' for another, it does not occur without intention. As for 'and you', one divorce occurs like his saying 'this one is divorced and this one' — it occurs upon her. And if he said 'this one and this one are divorced', both are divorced. And if he said 'this one, this one, divorced', the first is not divorced unless he says 'two divorced.' And if he said 'this one is divorced, this one', it does not occur upon the other without intention. And if he said to them 'you, then you, then you are divorced', the last is divorced; similarly with the letter 'and'. And if he said 'divorcées', they are all divorced. And if he gave the divorce [word] first, they are divorced. And if he said 'this one is divorced with you', it does not occur upon the addressed one except with intention. And what if he called his wife and another answered? That will come later.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the effect of conjunctions when pronouncing divorce on multiple wives. The key distinction is between 'then' (ثم) and 'and' (و). With 'then', each pronouncement is separate and only the last one takes effect because 'then' indicates sequence and separation. With 'and', the pronouncement is connected and affects all wives mentioned. For example, if a man says to his four wives 'You, then you, then you, then you are divorced', only the fourth is divorced. If he used 'and', all would be divorced. The passage also gives detailed cases: if he says 'You are divorced one and one', only one divorce occurs; if he says 'You are divorced and you', two occur (if addressed to different women); various combinations with 'and' and 'then' are explored. The principle is that 'and' (و) is for joining, so it connects the pronouncements; 'then' (ثم) cuts off the connection, so the divorce applies only to the last mentioned.

Key Terms
ثم (thumma): Conjunction meaning 'then'; in divorce pronouncements, it disconnects the statements, so only the last takes effect.
الواو (al-waw): Conjunction meaning 'and'; in divorce, it joins the statements, so the divorce applies to all mentioned unless intention specifies otherwise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his three wives: 'You, then you, then you are divorced.' How many wives are divorced?
A) All three
B) The first two
C) Only the last one
D) None

Key Takeaway
'Then' (ثم) severs the connection in divorce pronouncements, restricting the effect to the last mentioned; 'and' (و) joins them, affecting all mentioned.

Ruling on Unspecified Wife in Divorce Pronouncement
Explains the ruling when a husband says 'my wife is divorced' without naming, and he has one or more wives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband says 'my wife is divorced' without naming, and apply the rules of specification (bayan) when he has multiple wives.

Core Idea
If a husband has a known wife, the divorce takes effect by istihsan; if multiple, he may choose.

This lesson examines a common scenario in divorce: a husband utters a general pronouncement without specifying which wife he intends. The Hanafi school provides clear guidance, balancing the literal ambiguity with the reality of marital relations. We will explore the rulings for one known wife, multiple wives, and conditional oaths, using equity (istihsan) and specification (bayan).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Determine whether the husband has one or multiple wives, and whether the pronouncement is immediate or conditional.
The ruling changes dramatically based on these facts.
2
Apply Istihsan for a Known Wife
If he has one well-known wife, the divorce is effective despite the lack of naming; this is based on istihsan (equity) rather than strict qiyas.
Without naming, technically the pronouncement is ambiguous, but custom and context identify the wife.
3
Handle Multiple Wives
If he has two or more equally known wives, the divorce is not immediately applied; he may specify which one he intended (bayan).
His claim of intention is accepted, as the pronouncement was ambiguous.
4
Analyze Conditional Oaths
In a conditional divorce (e.g., 'if I do X, my wife is divorced'), if he has multiple wives, one divorce occurs upon the condition, and he can specify later. If he had already divorced one irrevocably and her iddah ended before the condition, the other is automatically specified.
The rule of bayan applies, but prior divorce changes the default.
5
Apply the Rule of Proof
If the husband claims he intended another wife (not the known one), his word is not accepted without evidence; the known wife is divorced by default.
This prevents manipulation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي موضع آخر منها : لو قال امرأته طالق ولم يسم وله امرأة معروفة طلقت استحساناً، ولو قال لي امرأة أخرى وإياها عنيت لا يقبل قوله إلا أن يقيم البينة. ولو قال امرأته طالق وله امرأتان كلتاهما معروفة كان له أن يصرف الطلاق إلى أيتهما شاء. وفي البزازية من الأيمان إن فعلت كذا فامرأته طالق وله امرأتان أو أكثر طلقت واحدة والبيان إليه، وإن طلق إحداهما بائناً أو رجعياً ومضت عدتها ثم وجد الشرط تعينت الأخرى للطلاق، وإن كان لم تنقض العدة فالبيان إليه ا هـ . وفي الخانية : ولو قال لامرأتي علي ألف المناسب . قوله : (لأن التعريف لا يحصل بالتسمية كذا في بعض النسخ وفي بضعها «بالنسبة» وهو
And in another place of it: if he said 'my wife is divorced' and did not name, and he has a known wife, she is divorced by istihsan. And if he said 'I have another wife and I intended her', his statement is not accepted unless he brings proof. And if he said 'my wife is divorced' and he has two wives, both known, he may direct the divorce to whichever he wishes. And in al-Bazzaziyyah from the section on oaths: if he said 'if I do such-and-such, my wife is divorced' and he has two wives or more, one is divorced and the specification belongs to him. And if he divorces one of them irrevocably or revocably and her waiting period passes, then the condition occurs, the other becomes specified for divorce. And if the waiting period has not yet ended, the specification belongs to him. And in al-Khaniyya: and if he said to my wife I owe a thousand... appropriate. His statement: (Because identification is not achieved by naming) so in some copies, and in some of them 'by relation', and it is...
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage covers cases where a husband says 'my wife is divorced' without naming any woman. According to the Hanafi school, if he has one known wife, the divorce applies to her by istihsan (equity). If he claims he intended another wife he also has, that claim is not accepted without proof. If he has two known wives, he may choose which one he meant. The passage also discusses a conditional divorce: if he says 'if I do this, my wife is divorced' and he has multiple wives, one divorce occurs and he may specify which one he intended. However, if he has already divorced one of them irrevocably (ba'in) and her waiting period has passed before the condition is fulfilled, then the other wife becomes the one that is divorced when the condition occurs. The marginal note at the end references a textual variant about identification by naming versus relation.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Equity; in this case, the divorce applies to the known wife based on istihsan, not strict qiyas.
البيان (al-bayan): Specification; the husband has the right to specify which wife he intended if he has multiple.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both are divorced
B) Neither is divorced until he specifies
C) He may choose which one he meant
D) The eldest is divorced

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an ambiguous divorce pronouncement is resolved by istihsan for a known wife or by bayan for multiple wives, ensuring the divorce is effective while protecting the husband's intention.

Effect of Pointing vs. Description in Divorce
Hanafi ruling on divorce when description is false but pointing identifies the wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce when the husband gives a false description but points to his wife, and explain why pointing overrides the description.

Core Idea
Pointing overrides false descriptions in divorce identification.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq clarifies a subtle point of Hanafi divorce law: when a husband describes his wife with an attribute that is false, the divorce is generally ineffective. However, if he accompanies the description with a pointing gesture, the pointing becomes the decisive factor. Understanding this rule prevents divorces from being incorrectly annulled due to mere linguistic errors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the legal question
We are dealing with a husband pronouncing divorce while describing his wife with an attribute that does not apply to her (e.g., calling his sighted wife 'blind'). The question is whether such a divorce is effective.
2
Apply the rule for description alone
If the husband uses only a description and that description is false, the divorce does not occur. The identification fails because the description does not match the actual wife.
The description is the sole identifier.
3
Add the element of pointing
If the husband points to a specific woman while pronouncing the divorce, the pointing becomes the primary identifier. Even if the description is false, the pointing determines the intended wife.
Pointing is a strong, visible identifier.
4
Derive the ruling
Therefore, when the husband says 'My blind wife is divorced' while pointing to his sighted wife, the divorce is effective on the sighted wife. Both the naming (tasmiyah) and the description (ṣifah) are disregarded in the presence of pointing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طلقت قضاء وديانة، ولو قال امرأته الحبشية طالق وامرأته ليست بحبشية لا يقع، ولو كان له امرأة بصيرة فقال امرأته هذه العمياء، طالق وأشار إلى البصيرة تطلق البصيرة ولا تعتبر التسمية ولا الصفة. مع الإشارة ا هـ .
It is effective in both judicial and religious contexts. If he said, 'My Abyssinian wife is divorced' but his wife is not Abyssinian, it does not occur. If he had a sighted wife and said, 'My blind wife is divorced,' pointing to the sighted one, the sighted one is divorced, and neither the naming nor the description is considered. With pointing, [the ruling is as such]. End quote.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage from the commentary addresses the priority of physical indication (pointing) over verbal description in divorce pronouncements. The ruling is that if a husband describes his wife with an attribute that does not apply to her, such as 'Abyssinian' when she is not, the divorce does not take effect because the identification is based on the description. However, if he adds a pointing gesture to a specific woman, the pointing becomes the decisive factor. Thus, if he says 'my blind wife' while pointing to his sighted wife, the sighted wife is divorced. This is because pointing provides a concrete identification that overrides any verbal description or naming. The Hanafi school emphasizes that the actual referent is what matters, not the semantic content of the words.

Key Terms
الإشارة (al-ishārah): Pointing or physical indication used to identify the subject of the divorce pronouncement.
التسمية (al-tasmiyah): Naming; using a specific name to identify the wife.
الصفة (al-ṣifah): Description or attribute used to identify the wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says to his sighted wife, 'My blind wife is divorced,' while pointing to her, what is the ruling?
A) The divorce does not occur because the description is false.
B) The divorce occurs because pointing overrides the false description.
C) The divorce occurs only if he intended the description.
D) The divorce is suspended until he clarifies.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, pointing overrides any false description or naming when identifying the wife in a divorce pronouncement.

Unspecified Divorce with Known and Unknown Wives
Hanafi rulings on unspecified divorce declarations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband says 'my wife is divorced' without naming, distinguishing scenarios with one known wife, two known wives, and a claim of intent for an unknown wife.

Core Idea
An unspecified divorce defaults to the known wife by istihsan; a claim to intend another wife requires proof.

When a husband says 'my wife is divorced' without naming, uncertainty arises. Hanafi jurists apply istihsan to resolve this: if he has one known wife, the divorce takes effect on her. If he claims he meant another (unknown) wife, his word is not accepted without evidence. If he has two known wives, he may choose which one to divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and number of wives
Determine what the husband said and how many wives he has. The ruling depends on whether his wives are known (named) or unknown.
2
Apply istihsan for a single known wife
If he has one known wife, the divorce falls on her by istihsan — overriding the strict requirement of specification.
Istihsan is used to avoid uncertainty and follow common practice.
3
Examine a claim of intent for an unknown wife
If he claims he intended another (unknown) wife, his statement is not credited; he must provide evidence to prove that other wife exists.
This prevents a husband from arbitrarily denying the divorce to his known wife.
4
Handle two known wives
If he has two known wives, he has the right to assign the divorce to either of them, because both are within the scope of 'my wife'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي موضع آخر منها : لو قال امرأته طالق ولم يسم وله امرأة معروفة طلقت استحساناً، ولو قال لي امرأة أخرى وإياها عنيت لا يقبل قوله إلا أن يقيم البينة. ولو قال امرأته طالق وله امرأتان كلتاهما معروفة كان له أن يصرف الطلاق إلى أيتهما شاء. وفي البزازية من الأيمان إن فعلت كذا فامرأته طالق وله امرأتان أو أكثر طلقت واحدة والبيان إليه، وإن طلق إحداهما بائناً أو رجعياً ومضت عدتها ثم وجد الشرط تعينت الأخرى للطلاق، وإن كان لم تنقض العدة فالبيان إليه ا هـ .
And in another place from it: If he said, 'My wife is divorced' and did not name, and he has one known wife, she is divorced by istihsan. If he said, 'I have another wife and I meant her,' his statement is not accepted unless he provides evidence. If he said, 'My wife is divorced' and he has two known wives, he has the right to assign the divorce to whichever of them he wishes. In Al-Bazzaziyyah from the chapters of oaths: If he said, 'If I do such and such, my wife is divorced,' and he has two or more wives, one divorce occurs, and the clarification is his. If he divorced one of them irrevocably or revocably and her waiting period passed, then the condition occurred, the other wife is specified for the divorce. If the waiting period had not expired, the clarification is his.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation where a husband says 'my wife is divorced' without specifying a name. By istihsan (juristic preference), if he has one known wife, the divorce takes effect on her because the phrase defaults to the known wife. If he claims he intended another unknown wife, his claim is not accepted without proof. If he has two known wives, he may choose which one to divorce. In the context of conditional oaths (e.g., 'If I do X, my wife is divorced'), if the condition is fulfilled, one divorce occurs, and the husband can clarify which wife he intended. However, if he had previously divorced one wife and her waiting period ended, then the other wife is automatically the one intended for the second divorce. This is because the first divorce has already been executed and the waiting period has closed the possibility of revocation.

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference used to rule that the divorce goes to the known wife by default.
بيان (bayān): Clarification; the husband's right to specify which wife he intended for the divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No divorce occurs because the phrase is ambiguous.
B) The divorce occurs on the known wife by istihsan.
C) He must specify which wife he intended within three days.
D) The divorce is valid only if he says her name.

Key Takeaway
When a husband says 'my wife is divorced' without naming, the known wife is divorced by istihsan, and his later claim to intend another wife requires proof.

Conditional Divorce Oaths with Multiple Wives
Hanafi ruling on conditional divorce oaths with multiple wives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for conditional divorce oaths involving multiple wives, including the effect of a prior divorce and the expiration of iddah.

Core Idea
One divorce occurs upon condition; husband clarifies unless a prior divorce and iddah expiry fix the intended wife.

When a husband swears a conditional divorce (e.g., 'If I do X, my wife is divorced') and has multiple wives, one divorce takes effect when the condition is fulfilled. The husband can clarify which wife he intended. However, if he had previously divorced one wife and her waiting period (iddah) ended before the condition occurred, then the other wife is automatically the one divorced — because the first divorce is final and cannot be revoked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the triggering condition
The husband says 'If I do such and such, my wife is divorced.' Determine if the condition occurred. Then note how many wives he has.
2
Apply the basic rule: one divorce occurs, clarification to husband
If the condition is fulfilled, one divorce becomes effective. The husband has the right to clarify which wife he intended by his oath.
This is because the phrase 'my wife' is ambiguous when there are multiple wives.
3
Consider a prior divorce and iddah expiry
If the husband had previously divorced one of his wives (either ba'in or raj'i) and her iddah has ended before the condition occurs, then the other wife is automatically the one meant for the conditional divorce.
Because the first divorce is complete and cannot be redirected.
4
Handle the case where iddah has not expired
If the prior divorce occurred but the iddah is still running at the time the condition is fulfilled, the husband retains the right to clarify which wife he intended.
The possibility of revocation (in raj'i divorce) keeps the first wife still connected.

Source Text

Interplay of Debt Acknowledgment and Divorce Pronouncements with Multiple Wives
Rulings on combining debt and divorce statements with multiple wives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when a husband's claim to redirect a debt or divorce to another wife is accepted in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Husband's word accepted in financial matters, not in divorce redirection without evidence.

The passage from Al-Khaniyyah addresses situations where a husband makes statements about debt or divorce involving multiple wives. The key difference lies in the default identification: for debts, the husband's word is trusted because it is against his interest and can be verified; for divorce, the default is the known wife, and he cannot divert the divorce to another without proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of statement
Determine whether the statement is financial (debt) or personal status (divorce). This determines the default rule.
2
Determine the default wife
The known wife is the default. If the husband claims another wife, that claim must be proven for divorce, but for debt it is accepted.
3
Apply the rule to the scenario
For financial statements, the husband's claim of another wife is accepted. For divorce, it is not accepted without evidence.
4
Consider consummation effect
If there are two wives and no consummation, consecutive divorces cannot be combined on one wife; if consummated, they can be combined.
This affects the validity of the divorce count.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية : ولو قال لامرأتي علي ألف درهم وله امرأة معروفة فقال لي امرأة أخرى والدين لها كان القول قوله، ولو قال امرأتي طالق ولها علي ألف درهم فالطلاق والدين للمعروفة ولا يصدق في الصرف إلى غيرها، وكذا لو بدأ بالمال فقال لامرأتي علي ألف درهم وهي طالق، ولو قال امرأتي طالق ثم قال لامرأتي علي ألف درهم ثم قال لي امرأة أخرى وإياها عنيت صدق في المال ولا يصدق في الطلاق. ولو كان له امرأتان لم يدخل بهما فقال امرأتي طالق امرأتي طالق ثانياً، فإن قال أردت واحدة منهما لا يقبل، وكذا لو قال امرأتي طالق وامرأتي طالق ثانياً وكذلك العتق ولو كان دخل بهما فقال امرأتي طالق امرأتي طالق كان له أن يوقع الطلاقين على إحداهما ا هـ .
In Al-Khaniyyah: If he said, 'My wife owes me a thousand dirhams' and he has a known wife, then said, 'I have another wife and the debt is for her,' his statement is accepted. If he said, 'My wife is divorced and she owes me a thousand dirhams,' then the divorce and debt are for the known wife, and he is not believed if he diverts it to someone else. Similarly, if he started with property: 'My wife owes me a thousand dirhams and she is divorced.' If he said, 'My wife is divorced,' then said, 'My wife owes me a thousand dirhams,' then said, 'I have another wife and I meant her,' he is believed in the property but not in the divorce. If he had two wives with whom he had not consummated, and he said, 'My wife is divorced, my wife is divorced a second time,' then if he said, 'I intended one of them,' it is not accepted. Similarly, if he said, 'My wife is divorced and my wife is divorced a second time,' and likewise regarding manumission. If he had consummated with both, and he said, 'My wife is divorced, my wife is divorced,' he has the right to effect both divorces on one of them.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage deals with the interaction between financial acknowledgments and divorce pronouncements. The general rule is that a husband's word is trusted in financial matters (like debt) because it is a statement against his own interest and can be verified. However, regarding divorce, his word is not trusted to divert the divorce from the known wife to another, because divorce is a serious matter and the default identification is the known wife. The passage provides various scenarios: if he first says 'my wife owes me a thousand dirhams,' then later claims another wife, he is believed about the debt. But if he first says 'my wife is divorced,' then later claims another wife, his claim is not accepted for the divorce. The passage also addresses the status of divorces when there are two wives and no consummation: consecutive pronouncements count as separate divorces, and he cannot combine them on one wife. If consummated, he may combine both on one wife.

Key Terms
دين (dayn): Debt; a financial obligation owed to the husband.
دُخول (dukhūl): Consummation of marriage.
صرف (ṣarf): Diverting the divorce or debt from the known person to another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He is not believed.
B) He is believed for the debt.
C) He is believed for both.
D) He is believed only if he has witnesses.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a husband's word is accepted in financial acknowledgments but not in diverting divorce to an unknown wife without evidence.

Mispronunciations and Naming in Talaq
Rulings on severe mispronunciations of 'Taliq' and the validity of naming a woman 'Taliq'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on severely mispronounced divorce words and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on using 'taliq' as a personal name.

Core Idea
A word so mispronounced that custom doesn't recognize it cannot effect divorce, even with intent.

When a husband attempts to pronounce 'taliq' but mutilates the word, does the divorce count? And what if he names his wife 'Taliq' and then calls her that? This lesson unpacks these edge cases, revealing the Hanafi commitment to customary usage (urf) and the subtle internal disagreement among later scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First, note that the passage discusses two distinct issues: (1) deleting letters from 'taliq' (mispronunciations), and (2) using 'taliq' or 'mutallaqa' as a personal name. Understand the facts for each.
The mispronunciation part has two subcases: deleting only lam (taq) and deleting both lam and qaf (ta).
2
Extract the ruling for mispronunciations
In both subcases, the ruling is the same: the divorce does not occur, even if the husband intended divorce. The rationale is that custom ('urf) does not delete two letters from the end of a word; such utterances are not recognized as valid divorce pronouncements.
The principle at work: al-'urf qaw'id – custom is a basis for legal rulings.
3
Understand the naming dispute
Now shift to the naming issue. Al-Mahbubi in Al-Talqih held that 'taliq' and 'mutallaqa' are not valid personal names (they are not used as names), so if a husband says 'Taliq is divorced' addressing his wife named Taliq, the divorce does not occur. The relied-upon view in Al-Khaniyyah disagrees: the primary meaning of the word is 'divorced', so it triggers divorce regardless of naming.
The dispute is within the Hanafi school (intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf), not between schools.
4
Apply the mufta bihi
The stronger view is that the divorce does occur, because the word's common usage (divorce) prevails over the remote possibility of being a name. This aligns with the Hanafi maxim: al-haqiqah tulzimu al-majaz – the literal meaning takes precedence over metaphorical usage unless evidence indicates otherwise.
Thus, in fatwa, one should rule that the divorce takes effect when the word is uttered, even if the wife bears that name.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن حذف اللام فقط فقال أنت طاق لا يقع وإن نوى، ولو حذف اللام والقاف بأن قال أنت طا وسكت أو أخذ انسان فمه لا يقع وإن نوى لأن العادة ما جرت بحذف حرفين من آخر الكلام وأطلق في طالق ومطلقة فشمل ما إذا سماها به فإنه يقع بخلاف ما إذا سماه حراً وناداه، والفرق أن الحر اسم صالح فصحت التسمية به وهو اسم لبعض الناس، وأما المطلقة والطالق فليس اسماً صالحاً فلا تصح التسمية. كذا ذكر المحبوبي في التلقيح وهو ضعيف والمعتمد ما في الخانية من عدم الفرق
If he deleted only the lam and said, 'You are taq,' it does not occur even if he intended. If he deleted the lam and the qaf and said, 'You are ta,' and remained silent or someone covered his mouth, it does not occur even if he intended, because custom does not delete two letters from the end of speech. And [the author] made absolute in 'taliq' and 'mutallaqa' that if he names her with it, then it occurs, unlike the case where he names him 'free man' and calls him by it. The difference is that 'free man' is a valid name, so naming by it is valid, and it is a name for some people. But 'mutallaqa' and 'taliq' are not valid names, so naming by them is not valid. Thus mentioned Al-Mahbubi in Al-Talqih, but it is weak, and the relied-upon is what is in Al-Khaniyyah of no difference.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage covers two topics. First, the effect of severely mispronouncing 'taliq'. Deleting only the lam gives 'taq' (with qaf), which is not a recognized word for divorce, so no divorce occurs even with intention. Deleting both lam and qaf gives 'ta' (just the letter ta), which is even more remote; it does not count. The reason given is that custom does not allow deleting two letters from the end of a word, so the pronouncement is meaningless. The second topic is whether a man can validly divorce his wife by calling her 'Taliq' (as a name) or 'Mutallaqa' (as a name). Al-Mahbubi in Al-Talqih argues that these are not valid personal names in Arabic, so if a husband says 'Taliq is divorced' intending a woman named Taliq, it does not take effect because the name is not legitimate. However, the relied-upon view from Al-Khaniyyah is that there is no difference; the word 'taliq' primarily means 'divorced', so the pronouncement is automatically understood as a divorce regardless of whether it is also a name. This reflects the Hanafi principle that the common meaning prevails over the possibility of a name.

Key Terms
طاق (ṭāq): A mispronunciation of 'taliq' by deleting the lam, leaving a meaningless word.
طا (ṭā): A further mispronunciation by deleting both lam and qaf, leaving just the first letter.
تسمية (tasmiyah): Naming; here, the act of using 'Taliq' or 'Mutallaqa' as a personal name.
طالق (ṭāliq): A woman divorced; also used as a personal name in some contexts.
مطلقة (muṭallaqa): A woman divorced; feminine form of 'mutallaq'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Does not occur because it is not a valid name.
B) Does not occur because he intended to address her by name, not to divorce.
C) Occurs because the primary meaning of 'taliq' is divorce.
D) Occurs only if he also points to her.

Key Takeaway
Custom ('urf) determines which utterances are valid for divorce; mispronounced forms that deviate too far from customary usage are ineffective even with intent, while the word 'taliq' always triggers divorce regardless of its use as a name.

Divorce and Debt: The Irrelevance of Intention to Another Woman
Rulings on divorce with concurrent debt claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce when a husband also claims a debt from his wife and explain why intention to another woman is ineffective for divorce but may be considered for debt.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, 'my wife' in divorce always means the actual wife; intention to divorce another is legally irrelevant.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq presents rulings at the intersection of divorce and debt claims. The core issue is whether a husband's statement 'my wife is divorced' can be redirected to another woman by intention, especially when he also mentions a debt. The Hanafi position is that for divorce the referent is fixed as the known wife; for financial claims, intention may be considered due to the possibility of debts with multiple parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Parties
Recognize the husband, his known wife, and potential other women mentioned in the claim.
2
Determine the Default Referent
Understand that 'my wife' in a divorce pronouncement refers to the actual wife, not any other woman.
This is a key principle: the marital bond is unique.
3
Distinguish Between Divorce and Debt
Note that divorce is inherently tied to the spouse; debt is a financial obligation that can exist between any two parties.
4
Apply to Combined Statements
When both divorce and debt are mentioned together (e.g., 'my wife is divorced and she owes me'), both apply to the known wife; intention to another is rejected.
The simultaneity ties both to the wife.
5
Apply to Separate Statements
When divorce is pronounced first, then later a debt claim is made, the husband is believed regarding the debt (he can intend another woman) but not regarding the divorce.
The divorce has already taken effect on the wife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
درهم وله امرأة معروفة فقال لي امرأة أخرى والدين لها كان القول قوله، ولو قال امرأتي طالق ولها علي ألف درهم فالطلاق والدين للمعروفة ولا يصدق في الصرف إلى غيرها، وكذا لو بدأ بالمال فقال لامرأتي علي ألف درهم وهي طالق، ولو قال امرأتي طالق ثم قال لامرأتي علي ألف درهم ثم قال لي امرأة أخرى وإياها عنيت صدق في المال ولا يصدق في الطلاق
...dirham, and he has a known wife, so he said 'for me another wife and the debt is owed to her', then his statement is accepted. And if he said 'my wife is divorced and she owes me a thousand dirhams', then the divorce and the debt are for the known wife, and he is not believed when claiming it is for another. Likewise if he began with the money: 'my wife owes me a thousand dirhams and she is divorced'. And if he said 'my wife is divorced' then said 'my wife owes me a thousand dirhams' then said 'I meant another woman', he is believed regarding the money but not regarding the divorce.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage presents rulings from the matn on intersection of divorce pronouncement and debt claims. When a man has a known wife and makes a statement that could refer to another woman, the default is that he means his known wife. If he explicitly says 'my wife is divorced and she owes me money', both divorce and debt apply to the known wife; his claim to have intended another woman is rejected. However, if he separates the statements (first divorce, then debt), he is believed regarding the debt (since he can have debt on any woman) but not regarding the divorce (since divorce is tied to his wife). The key principle: in divorce, the word 'my wife' is interpreted as the actual wife, and intention to refer to another is ineffective.

Key Terms
المعروفة (al-ma'rufah): The wife known to be his wife in the context of the statement.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the word 'my wife' in a divorce pronouncement always refers to the actual wife; intention to divorce another woman is legally ineffective.

Divorce of Two Wives with Intention: Consummation Matters
Ruling on intention in multiple divorce pronouncements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a husband can intend both pronouncements of divorce for one wife when he has two wives, and distinguish the rule before and after consummation.

Core Idea
Intention to combine two divorces on one wife is accepted only after consummation.

In Hanafi fiqh, the effect of a husband's intention when pronouncing divorce depends on the marital state. This lesson examines a specific scenario where a man with two wives utters divorce twice, and whether he can mean both for one wife. The key factor is whether consummation has occurred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the default ruling
The husband says 'my wife is divorced' twice. The default is that each pronouncement applies to a separate wife unless a condition allows otherwise.
Each utterance is a separate divorce.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
The ruling differs based on whether the husband has consummated marriage with both wives or not. The source text specifies 'lam yadkhul bihima' (not consummated) and 'dakhala bihima' (consummated).
Consummation is the key variable.
3
Apply the ruling to the unconsummated case
If the marriage has not been consummated with both wives, the husband's intention to combine both divorces on one wife is not accepted. Each pronouncement is applied to a different wife, so both wives are divorced once.
The bond is considered weak; each wife gets one divorce.
4
Apply the ruling to the consummated case
If consummation has occurred with both wives, the husband's intention is accepted. He can have both divorces fall on the same wife, leaving the other wife untouched.
The marriages are fully established.
5
Note the parallel for manumission
The same rule applies to freeing slaves: if a man says 'my slave is free' twice intending both for one slave, it depends on whether he has had physical relations with them (consummation-like).
Qiyas between talaq and itq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان له امرأتان لم يدخل بهما فقال امرأتي طالق امرأتي طالق ثانياً، فإن قال أردت واحدة منهما لا يقبل، وكذا لو قال امرأتي طالق وامرأتي طالق ثانياً وكذلك العتق ولو كان دخل بهما فقال امرأتي طالق امرأتي طالق كان له أن يوقع الطلاقين على إحداهما ا هـ
And if he had two wives and had not consummated marriage with them, and he said 'my wife is divorced, my wife is divorced' a second time, then if he said 'I intended one of them' it is not accepted. Likewise if he said 'my wife is divorced and my wife is divorced' a second time, and similarly for manumission. And if he had consummated marriage with both, and he said 'my wife is divorced, my wife is divorced', he is permitted to apply both divorces to one of them.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This ruling differentiates between unconsummated and consummated marriages. In an unconsummated marriage, when the husband repeats 'my wife is divorced' twice, he cannot intend both pronouncements to apply to a single wife; each is considered a separate divorce for each wife. Similarly for manumission. In a consummated marriage, he can intend both divorces for one wife, because the wives become distinct through physical relation. The reason: before consummation, the bond is weak and each pronouncement is taken separately.

Key Terms
دخل بهما (dakhala bihima): Consummated marriage with both wives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The intention is accepted; only one wife is divorced.
B) The intention is not accepted; both wives are divorced.
C) Only the first pronouncement counts; the second is invalid.
D) The divorce is suspended until consummation.

Key Takeaway
Intention to combine multiple divorces on one wife is only effective when the marriage has been consummated.

Ambiguous Divorce Utterances: 'Fulanah Taliq' and Truncated 'Tal'
Rulings on vague divorce utterances including truncated forms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a husband's vague divorce utterance (such as 'Fulanah taliq' or 'anti tal') results in an effective divorce, based on pronunciation, context, and intention according to Hanafi law.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, whether an ambiguous divorce utterance takes effect depends on common usage, context, and the speaker's intention.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with husbands who use words that could mean divorce or something else. The Hanafi school has detailed rules for when such utterances count as explicit divorce and when intention is required. We will break down the key scenarios: saying 'Fulanah taliq' without naming, and truncating 'taliq' to 'tal'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance type
Determine whether the husband said 'Fulanah taliq' (without naming) or a truncated form like 'anti tal'.
2
Check pronunciation for 'tal'
If 'tal', check whether the lam has kasra (i.e., 'tal-i') or sukun. If kasra, it is explicit. If sukun, it is ambiguous.
3
Assess context and intention
For ambiguous utterances, consider whether the conversation was about divorce (mudhakarat al-talaq) or if the husband was angry. If so, it is explicit even without intention. Otherwise, intention is required.
4
Handle claims of alternative meaning
If the husband claims a different meaning (e.g., Turkish 'spleen'), his claim is not accepted judicially if the pronunciation is common for divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : لو قال فلانة طالق ولم يسم باسمها إن نوى امرأته يقع وإلا فلا، لأن فلانة اسم مشترك يتناول امرأته والأجنبية. وأطلق اللام في طالق فشمل ما إذا فتحها فإنه يقع لأنه مما يجري على لسان الناس خصوصاً في الغضب والخصومة، فلو كان تركياً وقال أردت به الطحال وفي التركية يقال للطحال طالق لا يصدق قضاء. كذا في الخانية. ولو حذف القاف من طالق فقال أنت طال فإن كسر اللام وقع بلا نية وإلا فإن كان في مذاكرة الطلاق والغضب فكذلك وإلا توقف على النية. كذا في الخانية. وفي الجوهرة: لو قال أنت طال لم يقع إلا بالنية إلا في حال مذاكرة الطلاق أو الغضب، ولو قال يا طال بكسر اللام وقع الطلاق وإن لم ينو ا هـ . وهذا هو الظاهر
And in al-Muhit: If he said 'Fulanah is divorced' without naming her name, if he intended his wife it occurs, otherwise not, because 'Fulanah' is a common noun covering his wife and a stranger. And he used the lam in 'taliq' with fatha, so it includes when he pronounces it with fatha, then it occurs because it is common among people, especially in anger and dispute. So if he was Turkish and said 'I meant the spleen' [in Turkish 'taliq' means spleen] it is not accepted judicially. Likewise in al-Khaniyya. And if he dropped the qaf from 'taliq' and said 'anti tal' then if he put kasra on the lam it occurs without intention, otherwise if he was in a discussion of divorce and anger similarly, otherwise it depends on intention. In al-Jawhara: 'anti tal' does not occur except with intention except in the context of discussing divorce or anger, and if he said 'ya tal' with kasra on lam, divorce occurs even without intention. This is the apparent view.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage covers multiple ambiguous utterances. First, saying 'Fulanah is divorced' without naming: the word 'Fulanah' could refer to his wife or any woman; so the ruling depends on his intention. However, if he utters 'taliq' with a fatha on the lam (i.e., 'talaq' or similar), divorce occurs because it is a common pronunciation, especially in anger. A special case: a Turkish man who says 'taliq' meaning the spleen (a homophone in Turkish) is not believed judicially. For deleting the qaf and saying 'tal': if he says 'anti tal' with a kasra on the lam (like 'tali' meaning divorcer? Actually 'tal' is an abbreviation), it is an explicit divorce form due to common usage; without kasra, divorce only occurs if in the context of divorce talk or anger, otherwise needs intention. The author notes that the view from al-Jawhara (that it needs intention except in those contexts) is the apparent (zahir) position.

Key Terms
طالق (with fatha) (taliq (with fatha)): Pronunciation of 'taliq' with a fatha on the lam; considered common in speech.
طال (tal): Abbreviation of 'taliq' by dropping the qaf; can be explicit if used in context.

Key Takeaway

Ruling on Truncated Divorce Pronouncements: 'Taq' and 'Ta'
Ruling on truncated divorce utterances

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that saying 'taq' or 'ta' does not effect divorce even with intention, and identify the Hanafi condition that a divorce word must be a recognizable form.

Core Idea
A divorce word so truncated that it loses its conventional form does not effect divorce, even if intended.

In the Hanafi school, the wording used for divorce must be a recognizable expression. If a husband omits letters from the word 'taliq' to the point that it no longer sounds like a divorce pronouncement, his utterance is ineffective. This lesson examines two such truncated forms: 'taq' (dropping the lam) and 'ta' (dropping both lam and qaf).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is saying 'anti taq' or 'anti ta' with intent to divorce. The ruling is that divorce does not occur.
Both utterances are ruled invalid regardless of intention.
2
State the basis for the ruling
The word is too truncated; custom ('urf) does not treat it as a divorce utterance. The Hanafi school requires the word to be a conventionally recognized divorce term.
Intention cannot compensate for an unrecognizable form.
3
Apply the principle to other truncations
Any truncation that removes the word's recognizable form will similarly be ineffective. The key is whether the word remains customary as a divorce term.
This is determined by 'urf, not mere resemblance.
4
Distinguish from valid incomplete words
If the truncation is minor and the word is still conventionally understood (e.g., 'tali' in some dialects), it may count. But 'taq' and 'ta' are beyond that threshold.
This lesson focuses on clearly invalid truncations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن حذف اللام فقط فقال أنت طاق لا يقع وإن نوى، ولو حذف اللام والقاف بأن قال أنت طا وسكت أو أخذ انسان فمه لا يقع وإن نوى لأن العادة ما جرت بحذف حرفين من آخر الكلام
And if he dropped only the lam and said 'anti taq', it does not occur even if he intended. And if he dropped the lam and qaf by saying 'anti ta' and fell silent or someone took his mouth, it does not occur even if he intended because custom has not extended to deleting two letters from the end of a word.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
When a word is so truncated that it loses its conventional form as a divorce pronouncement, divorce does not occur. Saying 'taq' (dropping the lam from 'taliq') does not effect divorce even with intention, because it is not a recognized form. Similarly, saying 'ta' (dropping both lam and qaf) is even more remote from the original word, so it also does not occur. The reason is that custom ('urf) does not treat such utterances as divorce; they are too abbreviated.

Key Terms
طاق (taq): Truncation of 'taliq' by dropping the lam; not a valid divorce word.
طا (ta): Truncation of 'taliq' by dropping the lam and qaf; even more remote from the original word.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says 'anti taq' intending divorce. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) Divorce occurs if he intends it.
B) Divorce does not occur even if he intends it.
C) Divorce occurs only if he repeats it three times.
D) Divorce is suspended until he pronounces it fully.

Key Takeaway
Saying 'taq' or 'ta' does not constitute a valid divorce utterance in the Hanafi school because custom does not recognize such truncated forms.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
12 min
Naming a Wife 'Taliq' or 'Mutallaqah': Does It Effect Divorce?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on naming with divorce words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi position on whether calling your wife 'taliq' as a name effects divorce, and explain the significance of tarkhim in divorce utterances.

Core Idea
A name is not a divorce pronouncement; even 'taliq' as a name does not effect divorce in the mu'tamad view.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq tackles a subtle issue: if a husband addresses his wife as 'Ya Taliq' (O divorced one) as a nickname, does a divorce occur? The ruling hinges on whether the word is used as a name or as a pronouncement. We explore the intra-Hanafi disagreement and the later reliance on al-Khaniyya, and then we examine a related debate on truncated words like 'tal' (without qaf) and the concept of tarkhim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Issue
Recognize that the passage addresses whether calling one's wife 'taliq' or 'mutallaqah' as a name constitutes a divorce pronouncement. The initial statement suggests it does, but the relied-upon view rejects this.
The issue is purely linguistic and intentional: is the word a name or a divorce declaration?
2
Trace the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Identify the two positions: (1) al-Mahbubi in al-Talqih argues that 'taliq' is not a valid personal name, so using it as a name does not negate its literal meaning—divorce occurs. (2) The mu'tamad view from al-Khaniyya holds that naming with such words does not effect divorce; the word is treated as a name.
Note the analogy with 'hurr' (free) which is a valid name and thus does not effect manumission.
3
Understand the Tarjih
The author declares the view from al-Khaniyya as the relied-upon (mu'tamad) and calls al-Mahbubi's view weak (da'if). The tarjih is based on the principle that a name nullifies the literal divorce meaning.
The tarjih authority is the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq, citing al-Khaniyya.
4
Analyze the Tarkhim Digression
The passage then discusses truncated forms like 'tal' (from 'taliq') and whether dropping the qaf constitutes tarkhim. Al-Fath rejects tarkhim outside vocative, while al-Nahr defends it as linguistically valid. The author concludes that without the qaf, the word is not explicit, and the utterance should not be considered divorce even with intention.
This digression shows how linguistic principles affect fiqh rulings.
5
Apply the Ruling to Spelling Out Letters
The passage ends with a comparison to spelling out divorce letters (e.g., 'T-L-Q'). The same reasoning applies: it is not a valid pronouncement.
Both truncated and spelled forms fail to be clear divorce utterances.

وأطلق في طالق ومطلقة فشمل ما إذا سماها به فإنه يقع بخلاف ما إذا سماه حراً وناداه، والفرق أن الحر اسم صالح فصحت التسمية به وهو اسم لبعض الناس، وأما المطلقة والطالق فليس اسماً صالحاً فلا تصح التسمية. كذا ذكر المحبوبي في التلقيح وهو ضعيف والمعتمد ما في الخانية من عدم الفرق، وهو: وه قوله: (ولم يسم باسمها أي بأن ذكر لفظ فلانة المكنى به عن العلم لا الاسم العلم كما يدل عليه التعليل تأمل قوله : ( ولو حذف القاف من طالق الخ) وجه الوقوع بأنه ترخيم. قال في الفتح : غلط لأنه إنما يكون اختياراً في النداء في غيره اضطراراً في الشعر. قال في النهر وأقول: الترخيم لغة يقال على مطلق الحذف كما نص عليه الجوهري وغيره وهو الم المراد هنا ا هـ فتأمله . قلت : وفي كنايات الفتح والوجه إطلاق التوقف على النية مطلقاً لأنه بلا قاف ليس صريحاً بالاتفاق لعدم غلبة الاستعمال ولا الترخيم لغة جائز في غير النداء فانتفى لغة وعرفاً فيصدق قضاء اليمين. هذا في حالة الرضا وعدم مذاكرة الطلاق أما في أحدهما فيقع قضاء أسكنها أو لا وفيه أيضاً النظر المذكور لأنه إيقاع بلا لفظ له ولا لأعم منه ليكون كناية ليس بمجاز فيه، وهذا البحث يوجب أ أن لا يقع به أصلاً وإن نوى ومثل هذا البحث يجري في التطليق بالتهجي كـ أنت ط ل قه لأنه ليس
And he generally applied it to 'taliq' and 'mutallaqah' so that if he named her with it, divorce occurs, unlike when he named him 'free' and called him by it; the difference is that 'free' is a valid name, so naming with it is valid, and it is a name for some people, whereas 'mutallaqah' and 'taliq' are not valid names, so naming is not valid. Thus mentioned al-Mahbubi in al-Talqih, but it is weak, and the relied-upon is what is in al-Khaniyya of no difference. And it is: His saying: (and he did not name her with her name) meaning that he mentioned the word 'fulanah' which is a kinayah for the proper noun, not the proper noun itself, as indicated by the reasoning. Ponder his saying: (and if he dropped the qaf from 'taliq') the reason for occurrence is that it is a truncation (tarkhim). He said in al-Fath: this is a mistake because tarkhim is only optional in vocative, otherwise it is poetic necessity. He said in al-Nahr: and I say: tarkhim linguistically means absolute deletion as stated by al-Jawhari and others, and that is what is intended here. So ponder that. I say: In the book of kinayat in al-Fath, the apparent view is to suspend on intention absolutely because without qaf it is not explicit by consensus due to lack of common usage, and tarkhim linguistically is not permissible in non-vocative, so it is negated linguistically and customarily, so he is believed judicially. This is in a state of contentment and absence of divorce discussion; otherwise in one of them it occurs judicially whether he makes it silent or not, and there is also the aforementioned consideration because it is an occurrence without a word for it nor something more general to be a kinayah, and such research necessitates that it does not occur at all even with intention, and similar research applies to pronouncing divorce by spelling out letters like 'anta T L Q' because it is not...
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage deals with two issues: (1) naming a wife 'taliq' or 'mutallaqah' as a name; (2) the ruling on truncated forms (like 'tal' without qaf) and the concept of tarkhim. The initial statement says that if a man calls his wife 'taliq' or 'mutallaqah' as a name (e.g., 'Ya Taliq'), divorce occurs. Some scholars (like al-Mahbubi) draw a distinction: if he calls her 'free' (hurr) as a name, it does not effect manumission because 'free' is a valid name for humans, but 'taliq' is not a valid personal name, so calling her by it should be taken literally as a divorce pronouncement. However, the relied-upon (mu'tamad) view from al-Khaniyya is that there is no difference: naming with such words does not effect divorce. The commentary then discusses the rationale for the truncated form 'tal' (dropping qaf): some say it is tarkhim (truncation). Al-Fath criticizes this because tarkhim in Arabic is used for vocative, not for non-vocative. But al-Nahr defends it as linguistically valid. The author (in the 'I say' section) argues that without the qaf, the word is not explicit by consensus, and tarkhim is not permissible outside vocative, so the utterance should not be considered divorce at all, even with intention. He compares it to spelling out the letters (t-t-l-q) which is also not a valid utterance.

Key Terms
الترخيم (al-tarkhim): Linguistic truncation: dropping letters from the end of a word. Here debated whether it applies to non-vocative contexts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, if a man calls his wife 'Ya taliq' as a nickname, what is the ruling?
A) Divorce occurs because 'taliq' is not a valid name.
B) Divorce occurs only if he intends divorce.
C) Divorce does not occur.
D) The marriage is suspended pending a fatwa.

Key Takeaway
When a divorce word is used as a name, it does not effect divorce in the relied-upon Hanafi position; the context of naming overrides the literal meaning.
